Observation 1 - Chapter 2: Section 1: Goal 6: The remaining celestial spheres. Mercury, then opposite to that, so at conjunction in the tail and it passes beyond to the southern half moving away, then it concurs while passing beyond to the other half, which was northern and has now become southern. So its epicycle center is always either on the circle or south of it. Then for them (i.e., for Venus and Mercury) there are two other latitudes different from their latitude due to the orbit of their epicycle center in the manner we have depicted. For the diameter of their epicycle (passing through the apogee and perigee) coincides sometimes with the circle and separates from it at other times—as if he meant by 'the circle' the orbit of the epicycle center, for the coincidence of that diameter is only upon it at the midpoint between the two nodes, not at the ecliptic at the two nodes since there it is at extreme inclination from the orbit and the coincident ecliptic. Therefore a southern latitude is possible for the body of Venus and a northern latitude for the body of Mercury, as we have indicated. And likewise the diameter passing through the mean distances of their epicycle, intersecting the diameter passing [through the apogee and perigee] also. No consideration here also for the mover moving the deferent center. For such a state occurs in the epicycle center of Mercury and there is no other mover like the director so that its motion is considered. His saying: 'due to the orbit of their epicycle center' is connected to his saying: 'to their latitude', not to his saying: 'different'. And his saying: 'upon it' means upon the orbit of their epicycle center. And his saying: 'for it' means that diameter passing through the apogee and perigee. And his saying: 'there' means at the two nodes. And his saying: 'at extreme inclination from the orbit etc.': So Venus, as long as its center descends from the apogee from one of the two northern halves, its apogee inclines north and its perigee south; and as long as it ascends from the perigee from the other half, its apogee inclines south and its perigee north, opposite to the first half. As for Mercury, as long as its center descends from the apogee, its apogee inclines south and its perigee north; and as long as it ascends from the perigee, its apogee inclines north and its perigee south. His saying: 'And also the diameter passing through the mean distances of their epicycle: the two mean distances here are the two opposite points on the circumference of the epicycle such that each one is equal with respect to the center of the world, and likewise the distance of each one from the center of that epicycle is equal; like the diameter passing through the mean distances intersecting the diameter passing through the apogee and perigee at right angles; it has two ends: one is the trailing end, i.e., the eastern end, called the longitudinal distance, and the other is the leading end, i.e., the western end, called the morning distance. If you know this, we say: when the epicycle center of the two lower planets, i.e., Venus and Mercury, reaches one of the two nodes, this diameter passing through the mean distances becomes coincident with the orbit of the epicycle center and the ecliptic, i.e., it is in their plane while they are coincident; and then one end of the diameter passing through the apogee and perigee becomes north and the other south, and this is its extreme inclination from the orbit of the epicycle center. Then the two diameters intersect at right angles. Then if the epicycle center departs from one of the two nodes, one end of each of the two diameters inclines north and the other inclines south, until when the epicycle center reaches the midpoint between the two nodes, one end of the diameter passing through the mean distances becomes north and the other south, and this is its extreme inclination from the orbit of the epicycle center. Then if the epicycle center departs from one of the two nodes, one end of each of the two diameters inclines north and the other inclines south, until when the epicycle center reaches the midpoint between the two nodes, one end of the diameter passing through the mean distances becomes north and the other south, and this is its extreme inclination from the orbit of the epicycle center. The one passing through the apogee and perigee also has an inclination that entails latitude, and its quality is recorded in their books.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the inclinations and nodal behavior of the epicycles of Venus and Mercury within the Ptolemaic model. The commentator begins by describing the specific behavior of Mercury's epicycle center, noting that it is always either on the circle (the orbit of the center) or south of it, and that at conjunction in the tail it passes to the southern half. He then states that Venus and Mercury have two additional latitudes due to the orbit of their epicycle center. The key point is that the diameter through the apogee and perigee of the epicycle can coincide with the orbit of the center or separate from it. This coincidence occurs only at the midpoint between the two nodes (the points where the epicycle's plane intersects the ecliptic). At the nodes themselves, the diameter is at extreme inclination relative to the orbit and the coincident ecliptic, which allows Venus to have a southern latitude and Mercury a northern latitude. The commentator then explains the behavior of the other diameter, passing through the mean distances (the two opposite points on the epicycle circumference equidistant from the world center and from the epicycle center). This diameter intersects the apogee-perigee diameter at right angles and has two ends: the eastern (trailing) and western (leading) ends. When the epicycle center is at a node, the mean-distance diameter lies in the plane of the orbit and ecliptic, and the apogee-perigee diameter is perpendicular to it, with one end north and the other south, representing extreme inclination. As the epicycle center moves from a node towards the midpoint between nodes, the ends of both diameters incline alternately north and south until at the midpoint the mean-distance diameter has one end north and one south, again at extreme inclination. The commentator notes that this entire process is detailed in astronomical books. Throughout, he follows the original text's wording, explaining each phrase, such as 'due to the orbit of their epicycle center' being connected to 'to their latitude', and clarifying that the diameter through apogee and perigee is the subject of the inclination statements.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the apogee-perigee diameter always lies in the plane of the orbit.
Correction: The passage states that it coincides only at the midpoint between nodes; at nodes it is at extreme inclination.

Checkpoint
When does the diameter through the apogee and perigee coincide with the orbit of the epicycle center?
A) At the nodes
B) At the midpoint between nodes
C) At the apogee of the deferent
D) At the perigee of the deferent

Key Takeaway
The apogee-perigee diameter of the epicycle is aligned with the orbit only at the midpoint between nodes, and its inclination at nodes allows for the observed latitudes of Venus and Mercury.

Behavior of the Mean-Distance Diameter at Nodes
The commentator explains the mean-distance diameter and its relationship to the nodes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the behavior of the diameter through the mean distances of the epicycle and how its inclination changes as the epicycle center moves between nodes.

Core Idea
The mean-distance diameter and apogee-perigee diameter alternate inclinations as the epicycle center travels between nodes.

This passage delves into the second diameter of the epicycle, the one passing through the mean distances. It defines the two mean distances (eastern and western) and explains how this diameter interacts with the orbit and the ecliptic. When the epicycle center is at a node, this diameter lies in the plane of the orbit and ecliptic, while the apogee-perigee diameter is perpendicular to it. As the center moves away from a node, the ends of both diameters incline alternately north and south until at the midpoint they reverse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the translation carefully. Note the definitions of mean distances and the two ends.
The passage includes repetition for emphasis.
2
Visualize the motion
Imagine the epicycle center moving along its orbit. At a node, the mean-distance diameter lies in the orbit plane. As it moves to the midpoint, the ends incline.
Use a diagram if possible.
3
Connect to latitudes
Understand how the alternating inclinations contribute to the additional latitudes of Venus and Mercury.
The passage mentions this as recorded in books.

Factors Affecting Solar Eclipse Appearance
Describes how solar eclipse size depends on distances and lunar latitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon's distance from Earth and its visible latitude determine whether a solar eclipse appears total or annular.

Core Idea
Solar eclipses vary based on lunar distance and latitude.

This passage from the astronomical section explores the mechanics of solar eclipses. It explains why the moon sometimes covers the entire sun and other times leaves a ring of light. The key factors are the moon's distance from Earth and its celestial latitude. Understanding these principles helps explain the observed diversity of eclipse phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the two main topics: the effect of lunar distance and the effect of lunar latitude.
Pay attention to the terms حضيض (perigee) and أوجه (apogee).
2
Identify the two factors
The passage discusses how the moon's distance (perigee/apogee) affects its apparent size, and how its latitude relative to the sun affects coverage.
3
Apply to examples
Consider how a perigee moon (closer, larger) and an apogee moon (farther, smaller) would change the eclipse. Then consider how different latitudes affect whether a total, annular, or no eclipse occurs.
The passage gives a specific rule for latitude relative to the sum of radii.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثاني : المقصد الثالث : كسوف الشمس انكسافها (في حضيضها فلقربها ) منا ( ترى أكبر و يكون (القمر) حينئذ (في أوجه، فلبعده عنا ) يرى أصغر فلا يكسف جميع صفحتها بل تبقى منها حلقة نور محيطة به وقد روي أنها أي الحلقة النورانية ( رؤيت) على وجهها في بعض الكسوفات مع ندرته ( وإن كان للقمر ) في ذلك الاجتماع ( عرض ) مرئي ( فإن كان ) ذلك العرض ( بقدر مجموع نصف قطرهما لم يكسفها وإن كان أكثر منهما فبالطريق الأولي ) وإن كان أقل منهما كسفها بقدر ذلك كما لا يخفى )
First Observatory - Second Section: Third Objective: Solar Eclipse. Its eclipse (in its perigee because of its proximity to us) appears larger, and (the moon) then (is in its apogee, because of its distance from us) appears smaller, so it does not eclipse the entire surface of the sun, but rather a ring of light remains surrounding it. And it has been reported that it (i.e., the ring of light) was seen on its surface in some eclipses, though rarely. And if the moon has (in that conjunction) a visible (latitude), then if that (latitude) is equal to the sum of their radii, it does not eclipse it; if it is greater than that, then a fortiori; and if it is less than that, it eclipses it by that amount, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of solar eclipse. It begins by stating that when the moon is in its perigee (closest to Earth), it appears larger, and conversely when it is in apogee it appears smaller. As a result, during a solar eclipse, the moon does not entirely cover the sun; a ring of sunlight remains visible (annular eclipse). Then it addresses the effect of the moon's visible latitude during conjunction. If the moon's latitude equals the sum of the radii of the sun and moon, no eclipse occurs; if it is greater, certainly no eclipse; if it is less, the moon covers part of the sun proportional to that amount.

Key Terms
كسوف الشمس
حضيض
أوجه
عرض
الحلقة النورانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the moon must always exactly cover the sun during a solar eclipse.
Correction: The passage shows that the moon's distance and latitude cause variations, including annular eclipses where a ring of light remains, and partial eclipses depending on latitude.

Checkpoint
What determines whether the sun is completely eclipsed or an annular eclipse occurs?
A) The moon's phase
B) The moon's distance and latitude
C) The season
D) The Earth's rotation

Key Takeaway
The appearance of a solar eclipse depends on the moon's distance (affecting apparent size) and its latitude (affecting alignment), leading to total, annular, or no eclipse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the moon's distance and latitude precisely determine whether it blocks the sun's light. In your own life, what are the 'distances' (distractions, priorities) and 'latitudes' (minor compromises or sins) that determine how fully the light of truth and faith reaches your heart? Name one specific 'latitude' you have tolerated that dims that light, and commit today to correcting it.
?
The passage mentions a rare ring of light observed by astronomers. When was the last time you witnessed a rare moment of spiritual clarity or nearness to Allah that seemed to break through the usual 'eclipse' of heedlessness? What conditions made that moment possible, and how can you intentionally recreate those conditions?

Ibn al-Haytham's Theory of Lunar Phases and Its Refutation
Presents Ibn al-Haytham's theory of lunar phases and refutes it using lunar eclipse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Haytham's proposed explanation for lunar phases and why it is refuted by the occurrence of lunar eclipses.

Core Idea
If the moon were a spinning half-lit sphere, lunar eclipses could never happen.

Ibn al-Haytham offered a clever physical model for the moon's phases, suggesting the moon is a sphere half-illuminated that rotates. But a decisive observation—the lunar eclipse—shows this model cannot be correct. In this lesson, you will see the theory and its refutation side by side.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear parts: the theory and its refutation.
2
Identify Ibn al-Haytham's model
What does Ibn al-Haytham claim about the moon's nature and motion? Note the key elements: half-illuminated sphere, rotation about its own center.
3
Understand how the model explains phases
How does the rotation of a half-lit sphere produce the observed phases from new moon to full moon? Walk through the positions: opposition (full), conjunction (new), intermediate (crescent, etc.).
The model elegantly explains phases without Earth's shadow.
4
Grasp the refutation
Why does the lunar eclipse disprove Ibn al-Haytham's model? Consider: if the moon is always half-illuminated and rotating, would it ever enter Earth's shadow fully? The author argues it would not, yet eclipses occur.
This is a crucial example of empirical falsification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ابن الهيثم قال في اختلاف تشكلات القمر: إنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف لأن القمر كرة مضيئة نصفها دون نصف وأنها ) أي تلك الكرة ( تدور على مركز (نفسها بحركة مساوية لحركة فلكها فإذا كان نصفه المضيء إلينا) كما في حال المقابلة ) فبدر أو المظلم كما في حال المقارنة ) فمحاق وفيما بينهما يختلف قدر ما نراه من المضيء هلالياً ونصف دائرة وإهليلجياً ( ويبطله) أي يبطل قول ابن الهيثم ( ما ذكرناه من أمر (الخسوف ( فإن هذا الاحتمال يقتضي أن لا ينخسف القمر أصلاً )
And know that Ibn al-Haytham said concerning the variation in the shapes of the moon: It is possible that this variation is because the moon is a luminous sphere, half of it but not the other half, and that it (i.e., that sphere) rotates about its own center with a motion equal to the motion of its epicycle. So when its illuminated half is toward us, as in the case of opposition, it is a full moon; or the dark half, as in the case of conjunction, it is a new moon; and in between, the amount we see of the illuminated part varies as a crescent, half-circle, or oval. And it is refuted—i.e., Ibn al-Haytham's statement is refuted—by what we mentioned concerning lunar eclipse, for this possibility would entail that the moon would never be eclipsed at all.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents a theory by Ibn al-Haytham explaining the lunar phases. He proposed that the moon is a sphere that is only half-illuminated (like a half-lit ball), and it rotates on its axis with the same period as its orbital motion. Depending on the orientation relative to Earth, we see different portions of the illuminated half, producing phases from new moon to full moon. Then the author refutes this theory by pointing out that if the moon were always half-illuminated and rotating, it would never enter the Earth's shadow to cause a lunar eclipse, because the dark half could face us at conjunction, but the illuminated half would always be somewhere; the refutation relies on the fact that lunar eclipses do occur, contradicting Ibn al-Haytham's model.

Key Terms
ابن الهيثم
تشكلات القمر
كرة مضيئة
خسوف
المقابلة
المقارنة

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject Ibn al-Haytham's explanation of lunar phases?
A) It contradicts the lunar eclipse
B) It requires an extra epicycle
C) It cannot explain the crescent shape
D) It is based on faulty observation

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of lunar eclipses disproves Ibn al-Haytham's model because it would never occur if the moon were always half-illuminated and rotating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What step will you take today to align your beliefs with what Allah has taught us?

Opinion 1: The Dark Spots Are an Illusion
First opinion and refutation concerning the moon's dark spots as an illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first opinion—that the moon's dark spots are an illusion—is refuted based on the impossibility of widespread agreement on a single illusion.

Core Idea
If the dark spots were an illusion, observers would disagree; but they don't.

This lesson examines the first of six historical opinions about the dark spots on the moon. The opinion claims the spots are mere illusions with no reality. The refutation, however, points out a logical flaw: if they were illusions, observers would likely see different things, but they consistently see the same pattern.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the claim and the counterargument.
Notice the structure: 'qīla' (it is said) presents the opinion, and 'qulnā' (we say) introduces the refutation.
2
Understand the refutation logic
The refutation argues that if the spots were an illusion, different observers would have different perceptions, but all observers agree on the shape of the spots. This agreement proves the spots are real.
This is an argument from consensus (ijmāʿ) of observation.
3
Apply the reasoning
Think about other phenomena that might be considered illusions. How does consensus play a role in distinguishing reality from illusion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : آراء حول اختفاء القمر [ في محو القمر المشاهد في صحته وفيه آراء الأول : قيل : خيال ) لا حقيقة له (قلنا : فيختلف الناظرون فيه لاستحالة توافقهم كلهم في خيال واحد . (الثاني: قيل : هو (شبح ما ينطبع فيه من السفليات من الجبال والبحار) وغيرها . قلنا: ) فيختلف باختلاف القمر في قربه وبعده وانحرافه عما ينطبع فيه : الثالث : ) هو ( السواد الكائن في الوجه الآخر قلنا : فلا يرى متفرقاً ، الرابع : ( هو ) تسخين النار ) للقمر ( قلنا : مماس للنار ) لأنه مركوز في تدوير هو في ثخن حامل فبينه وبين النار بعد بعيد ولو فرض أنه في حضيض التدوير مع كونه في حضيض الحامل لم يتصور هناك مماسة إلا بنقطة واحدة ) ولا ) هو ( قابل للتسخن عندكم فكيف يتسخن بها ( الخامس : ) هو جزء منه لا يقبل (النور كسائر أجزائه القابلة له قلنا : فإذن لا يطرد القول ببساطة الفلكيات) إذ القمر حينئذ مركب من أجزاء متخالفة الحقائق. ( ويبطل ) على هذا التقدير ( جميع قواعد كم) المبنية على بساطتها . ( السادس :) هو (وجه القمر فإنه مصور بصورة إنسان ) أي بصورة وجه الإنسان فله عينان وحاجبان وأنف وفم. (قلنا : فيتعطل فعل الطبيعة عندكم لأن الكل عضو طلب نفع أو دفع ضر)
Fourth Objective: Opinions on the disappearance of the moon [concerning the dark spots observed on its surface]. It contains opinions. First: It is said: an illusion with no reality. We say: then observers would differ regarding it, because it is impossible for all of them to agree on a single illusion. Second: It is said: it is the reflection of what is imprinted in it from earthly things like mountains and seas, etc. We say: then it would vary with the moon's proximity, distance, and inclination relative to what is imprinted. Third: It is said: it is the blackness present on the other side. We say: then it would not be seen scattered. Fourth: It is said: it is the heating by fire of the moon. We say: it is not in contact with the fire, because it is set in an epicycle which is within the thickness of a carrier; between it and the fire is a great distance. Even if it were supposed to be at the perigee of the epicycle while at the perigee of the carrier, contact could only be at a single point, and it (the moon) is not capable of being heated according to you, so how could it be heated by it? Fifth: It is said: it is a part of it that does not receive light like the other parts that are receptive to it. We say: then the statement of the simplicity of the celestial spheres would not hold, because the moon would then be composed of parts with different natures, and on this assumption all the rules of (?) based on their simplicity would be invalid. Sixth: It is said: it is the face of the moon, for it is formed in the image of a human, i.e., like the human face, having eyes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth. We say: then the action of nature would be nullified according to you, because every organ is for attaining benefit or repelling harm.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage lists six opinions regarding the dark spots observed on the moon, each followed by a refutation. The first opinion claims the spots are an illusion; the refutation is that if it were an illusion, observers would disagree, but they consistently see the same pattern. The second says they are reflections of earthly features like mountains and seas; the refutation is that the reflection would vary with the moon's distance and orientation, but the pattern is stable. The third says the dark side is simply the far side; the refutation is that the spots appear scattered, not as a single dark area. The fourth attributes them to heating by the fire (presumably the celestial fire); the refutation is that the moon is far from the fire and not capable of being heated. The fifth claims that part of the moon does not accept light; the refutation is that this would contradict the simplicity of celestial bodies, implying composition and invalidating astronomical rules. The sixth says the spots form a face; the refutation is that if the moon were designed for a purpose, it would not have useless parts; every organ should have a function.

Key Terms
محو القمر
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dark spots are just optical illusions.
Correction: If they were illusions, observers would see different patterns, but they consistently see the same pattern, proving they are real.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation claim the dark spots cannot be an illusion?
A) Because illusions are impossible in astronomy
B) Because all observers agree on the pattern
C) Because the moon is perfectly smooth
D) Because the spots change over time

Key Takeaway
The consensus of observers on the moon's dark spots disproves the claim that they are mere illusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as 'just my imagination' even though others confirmed it? What does this passage teach you about the value of shared observation over private doubt?
?
Are there truths you avoid acknowledging because they challenge your assumptions about reality? If a group of sincere witnesses agrees on something, do you have the courage to accept it, even if it contradicts your own initial impressions?
?
Consider a time you insisted something was an illusion to avoid facing an uncomfortable reality. What would it mean for you today to accept the testimony of others as evidence of truth, even when it is hard?

Opinion 2: Reflection of Earthly Features
Second opinion and refutation: dark spots as reflections of earthly features.

Learning Objective
Understand why the second opinion—that the moon's dark spots are reflections of earthly features—is refuted by the argument that such reflections would change with the moon's position.

Core Idea
Reflections are not fixed; but the moon's spots are.

The second opinion suggests that the dark spots are reflections (shabaḥ) of earthly features like mountains and seas imprinted on the moon. The refutation points out that reflections would change as the moon moves closer, farther, or tilts, but the observed spots remain constant.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the claim and the counterargument.
2
Analyze the refutation
The refutation says the spots would vary (yakhtalif) with the moon's distance and inclination. Since they are stable, the opinion is invalid.
3
Consider the underlying principle
This refutation uses the principle of consistency: if the cause varies, the effect varies.

Opinion 3: The Far Side's Blackness
Third opinion and refutation: dark spots as the blackness of the far side.

Learning Objective
Comprehend why the third opinion—that the dark spots are the blackness of the moon's far side—is refuted by the observation that the spots are scattered, not a single dark area.

Core Idea
The far side is one dark area; but the spots are scattered.

The third opinion holds that the dark spots are simply the blackness of the moon's other side. The refutation counters that if that were the case, the dark area would appear as one contiguous region, but the spots are dispersed across the visible face.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the short text. The opinion is brief, and the refutation is direct.
2
Understand the visual argument
The refutation says the spots are seen mutafarriqan (scattered), not as a single blotch. This implies the far side is not the source.
3
Visualize the difference
If you look at the moon, you see multiple patches of darkness, not one big dark area. That supports the refutation.

Opinion 4: Heating by Celestial Fire
Fourth opinion and refutation: dark spots caused by fire heating the moon.

Learning Objective
Learn why the fourth opinion—that the dark spots are caused by the heating of the moon by fire—is refuted based on the distance and the nature of the moon.

Core Idea
The moon is too far from the fire to be heated, and it cannot absorb heat.

The fourth opinion attributes the dark spots to the scorching effect of a celestial fire. The refutation presents two main arguments: first, the moon is far from the fire, and even at the closest possible approach, contact would be minimal; second, according to the proponents' own cosmology, the moon is not capable of being heated.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the passage carefully. It contains detailed technical terms about celestial spheres.
2
Understand the distance argument
The refutation explains the moon's position in an epicycle and carrier, making it far from the fire. Even at perigee of both, contact is only at a point.
3
Consider the nature of the moon
According to the opponent, the moon is not receptive to heating (qābil li-l-tasakhkhun). So even if contact occurred, it would not heat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : آراء حول اختفاء القمر [ في محو القمر المشاهد في صحته وفيه آراء الأول : قيل : خيال ) لا حقيقة له (قلنا : فيختلف الناظرون فيه لاستحالة توافقهم كلهم في خيال واحد . (الثاني: قيل : هو (شبح ما ينطبع فيه من السفليات من الجبال والبحار) وغيرها . قلنا: ) فيختلف باختلاف القمر في قربه وبعده وانحرافه عما ينطبع فيه : الثالث : ) هو ( السواد الكائن في الوجه الآخر قلنا : فلا يرى متفرقاً ، الرابع : ( هو ) تسخين النار ) للقمر ( قلنا : مماس للنار ) لأنه مركوز في تدوير هو في ثخن حامل فبينه وبين النار بعد بعيد ولو فرض أنه في حضيض التدوير مع كونه في حضيض الحامل لم يتصور هناك مماسة إلا بنقطة واحدة ) ولا ) هو ( قابل للتسخن عندكم فكيف يتسخن بها ( الخامس : ) هو جزء منه لا يقبل (النور كسائر أجزائه القابلة له قلنا : فإذن لا يطرد القول ببساطة الفلكيات) إذ القمر حينئذ مركب من أجزاء متخالفة الحقائق. ( ويبطل ) على هذا التقدير ( جميع قواعد كم) المبنية على بساطتها . ( السادس :) هو (وجه القمر فإنه مصور بصورة إنسان ) أي بصورة وجه الإنسان فله عينان وحاجبان وأنف وفم. (قلنا : فيتعطل فعل الطبيعة عندكم لأن الكل عضو طلب نفع أو دفع ضر)
Fourth Objective: Opinions on the disappearance of the moon [concerning the dark spots observed on its surface]. It contains opinions. First: It is said: an illusion with no reality. We say: then observers would differ regarding it, because it is impossible for all of them to agree on a single illusion. Second: It is said: it is the reflection of what is imprinted in it from earthly things like mountains and seas, etc. We say: then it would vary with the moon's proximity, distance, and inclination relative to what is imprinted. Third: It is said: it is the blackness present on the other side. We say: then it would not be seen scattered. Fourth: It is said: it is the heating by fire of the moon. We say: it is not in contact with the fire, because it is set in an epicycle which is within the thickness of a carrier; between it and the fire is a great distance. Even if it were supposed to be at the perigee of the epicycle while at the perigee of the carrier, contact could only be at a single point, and it (the moon) is not capable of being heated according to you, so how could it be heated by it? Fifth: It is said: it is a part of it that does not receive light like the other parts that are receptive to it. We say: then the statement of the simplicity of the celestial spheres would not hold, because the moon would then be composed of parts with different natures, and on this assumption all the rules of (?) based on their simplicity would be invalid. Sixth: It is said: it is the face of the moon, for it is formed in the image of a human, i.e., like the human face, having eyes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth. We say: then the action of nature would be nullified according to you, because every organ is for attaining benefit or repelling harm.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage lists six opinions regarding the dark spots observed on the moon, each followed by a refutation. The first opinion claims the spots are an illusion; the refutation is that if it were an illusion, observers would disagree, but they consistently see the same pattern. The second says they are reflections of earthly features like mountains and seas; the refutation is that the reflection would vary with the moon's distance and orientation, but the pattern is stable. The third says the dark side is simply the far side; the refutation is that the spots appear scattered, not as a single dark area. The fourth attributes them to heating by the fire (presumably the celestial fire); the refutation is that the moon is far from the fire and not capable of being heated. The fifth claims that part of the moon does not accept light; the refutation is that this would contradict the simplicity of celestial bodies, implying composition and invalidating astronomical rules. The sixth says the spots form a face; the refutation is that if the moon were designed for a purpose, it would not have useless parts; every organ should have a function.

بساطة الفلكيات
تدوير
حامل
حضيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dark spots are burns from celestial fire.
Correction: The moon is too far from the fire and cannot be heated, so burns are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what prevents the moon from being heated by the fire?
A) The moon is made of ice
B) The moon is too distant and not receptive to heat
C) The fire is too weak
D) The moon's rotation cools it

Key Takeaway
The combination of great distance and the moon's incapacity for heating disproves the fire-heating theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you accepted an explanation without checking the assumptions behind it, like the assumption that the moon could be heated? Today, commit to examining one deeply held assumption about your faith or worldview that you have never questioned.
?
The moon's nature prevents it from being heated by external fire. What is something in your character that you have been trying to change through external means (people, circumstances) but that can only be transformed by an inner change from Allah? Will you sincerely ask Him to change your heart?
?
This refutation uses precise reasoning to dismiss a false opinion. Have you allowed comfortable but false opinions about Allah's attributes or His creation to persist in your mind because you haven't subjected them to rigorous thought? Set aside time this week to critically examine one belief you've never questioned.

Opinion 5: A Part That Does Not Receive Light
Fifth opinion and refutation: dark spots as a part that does not receive light.

Learning Objective
Grasp why the fifth opinion—that the dark spots are a part of the moon that does not receive light—is refuted by undermining the principle of celestial simplicity.

Core Idea
If part of the moon doesn't receive light, the moon is not simple—breaking a key principle.

The fifth opinion proposes that the dark spots are a region of the moon that is intrinsically incapable of receiving light, unlike the rest. The refutation argues that this would make the moon composed of parts with different natures, contradicting the doctrine of the simplicity of celestial spheres (basāṭa al-falakiyyāt). This would invalidate many astronomical rules based on that simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the text. Note how the refutation connects to broader philosophical principles.
2
Understand the principle of simplicity
Celestial spheres are considered simple (basīṭa), meaning they are uniform in nature. If the moon had parts that differ in their ability to receive light, it would be composite.
3
Appreciate the consequences
The refutation says this would invalidate all rules (qawāʿid) based on simplicity, showing the high stakes.

Source Text

Opinion 6: The Moon's Face Formed Like a Human
Sixth opinion and refutation: dark spots form a human face on the moon.

Learning Objective
Understand why the sixth opinion—that the dark spots form a human face on the moon—is refuted by appealing to the principle of purposeful creation (fiʿl al-ṭabīʿa).

Core Idea
An organ must have a purpose; a face on the moon would be useless.

The sixth opinion claims that the dark spots are actually the face of the moon, shaped like a human face with eyes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth. The refutation argues that every organ (ʿuḍw) in nature exists for a purpose: to attain benefit or repel harm. A mere facial image on the moon serves no such purpose, so it would be a vain creation, which contradicts the principle of nature's purposeful action.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and refutation
Read the short text. Notice the analogy to a human face.
2
Understand the teleological argument
The refutation uses the idea that natural things have functions. A face on the moon has no function, so it would be useless.
3
Consider the implication
If nature does nothing in vain, then the existence of a facial pattern without purpose is impossible.

Source Text

?

?

?

Refuting the Illusion Theory of Lunar Spots
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots are an illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi refutes the claim that lunar spots are an optical illusion using consensus of observers.

Core Idea
If lunar spots were illusion, observers would disagree; they don't, so it's real.

Al-Amidi begins by addressing the simplest explanation for the lunar spots: that they are merely an illusion with no real existence. This claim, if true, would have a logical consequence that can be tested. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and its translation. Note the concise structure: a claim followed by a rebuttal.
The passage is only two sentences, but packed with logic.
2
Identify the claim
The first opinion states that the lunar spots are an illusion (خيال) with no reality. This is the view being refuted.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Amidi argues that if it were an illusion, observers would necessarily differ in their perception, because it is impossible for everyone to agree on the same illusion. Yet in fact, all observers see the same pattern. Therefore, it cannot be an illusion.
The key point is that unanimity in perception disproves the illusion claim.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the logical form: a conditional (if illusion, then disagreement) whose consequent is false (no disagreement), so the antecedent must also be false. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
This method is common in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : قيل : خيال لا حقيقة له . قلنا : فيختلف الناظرون فيه لاستحالة توافقهم كلهم في خيال واحد .
First: It was said: an illusion with no reality. We said: So the observers would differ regarding it, because it is impossible for all of them to agree on a single illusion.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first opinion about the lunar spots. It is an objection-and-answer format. The opinion states that the spots are an illusion, having no real existence. The refutation argues that if it were an illusion, observers would disagree about it, but in fact they all see the same pattern, which proves it is not an illusion.

Key Terms
خيال
اختلاف الناظرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lunar spots are merely an optical illusion with no physical reality.
Correction: If they were an illusion, observers would perceive them differently, but everyone sees the same pattern, proving they are real.

Checkpoint
How does al-Amidi refute the claim that lunar spots are an illusion?
A) By stating that illusions cannot be seen by many people.
B) By arguing that observers would disagree if it were an illusion, but they all see the same pattern.
C) By proving the moon is made of fire.
D) By citing ancient authorities.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of observers on the appearance of lunar spots disproves the claim that they are an illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'illusions' in your life do you assume others see the same way, and what would it take to admit they might be subjective?
?
When has your agreement with a group kept you from questioning whether something is really true?
?
If you were the only one who disagreed with everyone else about something you see, would you trust your observation or the crowd? Why?

Second Opinion on Lunar Spots: Imprint of Earthly Objects
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots are imprints of earthly features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion about lunar spots and the rational refutation that disproves it.

Core Idea
Lunar spots are constant; if they were reflections, they would change.

Al-Amidi presents the second opinion that lunar spots are imprints of earthly features like mountains and seas. He then offers a succinct refutation based on the moon's varying distance and angle. This lesson examines both the claim and the counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the brevity of the refutation.
2
Identify the claim
What is the second opinion saying? It claims that lunar spots are images (شبح) of what is imprinted (انطباع) from lower things (سفليات).
The claim treats the moon like a mirror.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Amidi says: 'We said: So it would vary with the moon's nearness, farness, and deviation from what is imprinted in it.' Why does this refute the claim?
The refutation points out a necessary consequence that contradicts observation.
4
Reflect on the evidence
The spots appear fixed, not changing with the moon's position. Therefore, they cannot be imprints of earthly features.
This shows how rational argument is used to judge empirical claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قيل : هو شبح ما ينطبع فيه من السفليات من الجبال والبحار وغيرها . قلنا: فيختلف باختلاف القمر في قربه وبعده وانحرافه عما ينطبع فيه .
Second: It was said: It is the image of what is imprinted in it from the lower things, such as mountains, seas, and others. We said: So it would vary with the moon's nearness, farness, and deviation from what is imprinted in it.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second opinion. The opinion claims that the lunar spots are reflections or imprints of earthly features like mountains and seas, as in a mirror. The refutation argues that if this were the case, the spots would change depending on the moon's distance and angle relative to the earth, but they appear fixed.

Key Terms
شبح
انطباع
سفليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lunar spots are reflections of earthly features like mountains and seas.
Correction: The refutation shows that if they were reflections, they would vary with the moon's distance and angle, but they remain constant.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why can't the lunar spots be imprints of earthly features?
A) Because the moon is too far away.
B) Because they would change with the moon's distance and angle.
C) Because the moon is not a mirror.
D) Because the spots are too dark.

Key Takeaway
The constancy of lunar spots disproves the claim that they are imprints of earthly features.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the moon tonight. Its spots are constant, just as they were for your ancestors. Does that constancy strike you as a sign of Allah's precision? How does this push you to consider the constancy of His mercy and justice in your own life?
?
You just learned that a false claim was refuted by its own logical consequence: if it were true, the spots would change. Can you identify a false assumption you hold about Allah's creation or your own life that would similarly crumble under honest scrutiny? Will you check it today?
?
The argument here shows the danger of making claims without evidence. When was the last time you believed something about your faith or life without checking the evidence? How can you commit to verifying claims against the teachings of the Quran and sound reason?

The Dark Side Theory of Lunar Spots and Its Refutation
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots are the dark side of the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion regarding lunar spots—that they are the dark side of the moon—and why this view is invalid according to al-Amidi.

Core Idea
If lunar spots were the dark side, they'd appear scattered, not fixed.

Al-Amidi continues his survey of opinions about the dark spots on the moon. The third view proposes that these spots are simply the dark side of the moon seen through some transparency. However, al-Amidi offers a concise refutation based on the observed pattern of the spots. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Amidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the opinion and refutation
The passage presents a third opinion followed by a short refutation. Identify each part and see how the refutation directly responds to the claim.
3
Reflect on the refutation's logic
Consider why the refutation works: if the spots were the dark side, they would not have sharp boundaries but would appear scattered. How does this match what we observe on the moon?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : هو السواد الكائن في الوجه الآخر . قلنا : فلا يرى متفرقاً .
Third: It is the blackness occurring on the other face. We said: So it would not be seen as scattered.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion and its refutation. The opinion holds that the spots are the dark side of the moon visible through some transparency. The refutation is brief: if that were the case, the dark area would appear scattered or diffuse, not as distinct fixed spots. The implication is that the observed pattern is too well-defined to be the other side.

Key Takeaway
The third opinion fails because the dark side would not produce the fixed, discrete spots we see.

?

The Fourth Opinion: Heating of the Moon by Fire Refuted
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots are caused by fire heating the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Amidi argues that the moon cannot be heated by the celestial fire, based on Ptolemaic astronomy and the nature of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
Even if fire touches the moon, it cannot heat it – distance and celestial nature prevent it.

The fourth opinion tries to explain the lunar spots as burns from the fire sphere. Al-Amidi's refutation draws on the complex geometry of Ptolemaic astronomy and the fundamental properties of celestial matter. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall gist of the argument.
Notice the technical terms like تدوير (epicycle) and حامل (deferent).
2
Understand the opinion
The opinion claims that the dark spots on the moon are burns caused by the proximity of the celestial fire sphere.
This is the fourth opinion being refuted.
3
Examine the first refutation
Al-Amidi raises the question of contact. He argues that the moon is far from the fire because it is embedded in an epicycle within a deferent, creating a great distance.
4
Examine the second refutation
Even at the closest approach (perigee of both epicycle and deferent), only a single point of contact is possible, which cannot cause significant heating.
5
Connect to kalam
This refutation shows how empirical astronomy and physics inform theological arguments about the nature of celestial phenomena.
The moon's insusceptibility to heat is a key Aristotelian principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : هو تسخين النار للقمر . قلنا : مماس للنار لأنه مركوز في تدوير هو في ثخن حامل فبينه وبين النار بعد بعيد ولو فرض أنه في حضيض التدوير مع كونه في حضيض الحامل لم يتصور هناك مماسة إلا بنقطة واحدة ولا هو قابل للتسخن عندكم فكيف يتسخن بها .
Fourth: It is the heating of the moon by fire. We said: It is in contact with fire? Because it is fixed in an epicycle which is in the thickness of a deferent, so between it and the fire is a great distance. Even if it were supposed that it is at the perigee of the epicycle while also at the perigee of the deferent, contact would only be imagined at a single point, and it is not susceptible to heating according to you, so how could it be heated by it?
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth opinion and its refutation. The opinion claims the dark spots are burns from the celestial fire sphere. The refutation uses Ptolemaic astronomy: the moon is embedded in an epicycle within a deferent, far from the fire sphere. Even at closest approach, only a single point contact is possible, and the moon (being celestial) is not susceptible to heat. Thus heating is impossible.

Key Terms
تسخين
النار
تدوير
حامل
حضيض
مماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fire sphere directly touches the moon, causing burns.
Correction: The moon is far away due to the epicycle and deferent system, and even at closest approach only a single point touches; moreover, celestial bodies are not subject to heating.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Amidi reject the idea that fire heats the moon?
A) Because the moon is made of water.
B) Because the moon is far from the fire and not susceptible to heat.
C) Because fire does not exist in the heavens.
D) Because the spots are too small.

Key Takeaway
The moon's dark spots cannot be burns from fire because the moon is too far, only touches at a point, and is not receptive to heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim that seems widely accepted, do you ever pause to examine the evidence yourself, or do you follow the crowd? This lesson shows Al-Amidi questioning a common opinion with precise reasoning. Think of one belief you hold simply because others do—are you willing to investigate it critically today?
?
Al-Amidi uses both geometry (epicycle, deferent) and physics (insusceptibility to heat) to refute the argument. In your own search for truth, do you integrate different fields of knowledge, or do you rely on a single source? Commit to learning one thing from a discipline outside your comfort zone this month to deepen your understanding of reality.
?
The refutation hinges on the principle that the moon, being celestial, is not receptive to heat. How often do you assume that all things operate by the same rules? Are there areas in your faith or life where you project earthly limitations onto God's creation? Take a moment to thank Allah for the unique nature of each part of His creation and ask for guidance to know them as they truly are.

Fifth Opinion: Lunar Spots as Non-Luminous Part
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots are a part that does not accept light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth opinion regarding lunar spots—that they are a part that does not accept light—and al-Amidi's refutation based on the principle of celestial simplicity.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies are simple; a composite moon undermines all astronomy.

Al-Amidi now addresses the claim that lunar spots are a part of the moon inherently incapable of receiving light. He argues that if the moon had such a part, it would be composite with different natures, contradicting the foundational principle that celestial bodies are simple. This would invalidate the entire astronomical system built on that simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the claim: a part of the moon does not accept light, unlike other parts.
This is the fifth opinion.
2
Identify the refutation
Al-Amidi refutes by saying this would make the moon composite (murakkab) with differing natures, contradicting the simplicity (basata) of celestial bodies.
Simplicity is a key principle in Ptolemaic astronomy.
3
Consider the consequences
The refutation also states that all astronomical rules built on simplicity would be invalidated if such composition existed.
This shows the high stakes of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : هو جزء منه لا يقبل النور كسائر أجزائه القابلة له . قلنا : فإذن لا يطرد القول ببساطة الفلكيات إذ القمر حينئذ مركب من أجزاء متخالفة الحقائق. ويبطل على هذا التقدير جميع قواعد كم المبنية على بساطتها .
Fifth: It is a part of it that does not accept light, unlike the rest of its parts that accept it. We said: So then the statement about the simplicity of the celestial bodies would not hold, because the moon would then be composed of parts with differing natures. And on this assumption, all the rules of [astronomy] built on their simplicity would be invalidated.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth opinion and its refutation. The opinion claims that some part of the moon is inherently unable to receive light. The refutation argues that this would make the moon composite with different natures, contradicting the fundamental principle that celestial bodies are simple (non-composite). Since astronomy (possibly Ptolemaic) is based on this simplicity, the opinion would undermine its entire framework.

Key Terms
بساطة الفلكيات
مركب
قواعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think lunar spots could be a different substance or part that does not receive light.
Correction: Al-Amidi shows this would violate the principle of celestial simplicity, which is a given in this cosmological framework.

Checkpoint

A) The moon would become brighter.

C) The moon would be made of fire.
D) The spots would disappear.

Key Takeaway
The claim of a light-resistant part on the moon is invalid because it contradicts the essential simplicity of celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Amidi shows that a single small assumption—if allowed—can unravel an entire system of knowledge. If the principle of simplicity were abandoned, astronomy would collapse. What foundational Islamic principle would you be unwilling to abandon, even if every circumstance pressured you? Are you living in a way that shows that principle is truly your foundation?

The Face of the Moon: Refuting Anthropomorphic Lunar Features
Al-Amidi presents and refutes the claim that lunar spots form a human face.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's teleological refutation of the claim that lunar spots form a human face.

Core Idea
Purposeful organs cannot exist in a purposeless moon.

Al-Amidi presents a sixth opinion that the spots on the moon form a human face with eyes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth. He refutes this by applying the principle of teleology: every organ exists for a function, but the moon is incapable of any such function, which would make nature's design vain and imperfect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow settle before interpreting.
Notice the concise style: the claim is stated succinctly, then the refutation follows with a clear logical structure.
2
Identify the claim and its details
The claim is that the lunar spots form a human face with specific features: two eyes, two eyebrows, a nose, and a mouth. Note that the opinion takes the resemblance literally.
This is not a metaphorical resemblance but an assertion of actual design.
3
Understand the refutation's structure
Al-Amidi refutes by arguing that every organ in a living being has a purpose (mouth for eating, nose for smell, eyebrows for sweat diversion). The moon, being inanimate, cannot fulfill these purposes. So attributing such organs would make nature's action nullified (تعطيل فعل الطبيعة).
The refutation hinges on the principle that nature does nothing in vain – a key tenet of teleology.
4
Reflect on the theological principle used
The argument appeals to the idea of 'the best and most perfect system' (أحسن النظام). The claim of a lunar face would violate this perfection because it introduces purposeless organs, contradicting divine wisdom.
This shows how rational arguments about nature are used to support theological doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : هو وجه القمر فإنه مصور بصورة إنسان أي بصورة وجه الإنسان فله عينان وحاجبان وأنف وفم. قلنا : فيتعطل فعل الطبيعة عندكم لأن الكل عضو طلب نفع أو دفع ضر فإن الفم لدخول الغذاء والأنف لفائدة الشم والحاجبين لدفع العرق عن العينين، وليس القمر قابلاً لشيء من ذلك، فيلزم التعطيل الدائم فيما زعمتم أنه أحسن النظام وأبلغه .
Sixth: It is the face of the moon, for it is shaped in the form of a human, i.e., in the form of a human face, so it has two eyes, two eyebrows, a nose, and a mouth. We said: So the action of nature would be nullified according to you, because every organ is for seeking benefit or repelling harm. The mouth is for entry of food, the nose for the benefit of smell, the eyebrows for repelling sweat from the eyes, and the moon is not susceptible to any of that. So perpetual nullification would follow in what you claim is the best and most perfect system.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth opinion and its refutation. The opinion claims the spots form a human face with features like eyes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth. The refutation argues from teleology: every organ in a living being serves a purpose. But the moon, being inanimate, cannot have such functions, so attributing organs to it would imply nature's purpose is thwarted, contradicting the idea of a perfect creation. This argument relies on the principle that nature does nothing in vain.

Key Terms
وجه القمر
صورة إنسان
تعطيل فعل الطبيعة
أحسن النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lunar face is just a curious resemblance without implications.
Correction: Al-Amidi shows that if the spots were truly a face, the organs would have functions, but the moon cannot have them, so the claim contradicts nature's perfect design.

Checkpoint
How does al-Amidi refute the claim that the lunar spots form a human face?
A) He says the features are not clear.
B) He argues that organs imply purposes, but the moon cannot fulfill them, nullifying nature's wisdom.
C) He states that the moon has no face.
D) He says the spots are too small to be a face.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi refutes the lunar face claim by demonstrating that attributing organs to the moon would nullify the wisdom of nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the moon, do you see only a pattern of light and dark, or do you see the hand of a Creator who crafted every detail with purpose? How can you train your eyes to see wisdom in creation rather than settle for superficial explanations?
?
The passage argues that attributing organs without purpose would nullify nature's perfection. In your own life, are there 'organs'—talents, resources, or time—that you are not using for their intended purpose? What would it take to restore their purpose and align your life with divine wisdom?
?
If a theologian can refute a false claim by showing it contradicts the perfection of God's system, are you equally vigilant against beliefs or habits that subtly undermine your trust in God's perfect plan? What false assumptions about your own life might be hiding a similar contradiction?

The Seventh Opinion: Different Celestial Bodies
Al-Amidi presents the seventh opinion and evaluates it as closest but unreliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's presentation of the seventh opinion on lunar spots and his critical evaluation that it is the closest but still unreliable.

Core Idea
The most plausible opinion may still be uncertain.

After reviewing several opinions on the nature of the spots on the moon, al-Amidi presents the seventh and final opinion. He acknowledges that this one is the most convincing among them, yet he maintains his critical stance, refusing to accept it as definitively proven. As you read this passage, notice how he balances being drawn to a view with insisting on evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words speak directly.
2
Identify the description
Notice that the seventh opinion describes the spots as celestial bodies that are different from the moon, moving within its epicycle, not uniformly illuminated, and always maintaining a fixed relative position.
These three characteristics are key to understanding the proposal.
3
Analyze the evaluation
Focus on al-Amidi's judgment: 'closest' (أقرب) but 'not suitable for reliance.' Why would he say this? Consider that plausibility does not equal proof.
This reveals the author's methodological caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : هو أجسام سماوية مختلفة معه في تدويره غير قابلة للإنارة بالتساوي حافظة لوضعها معه دائماً . وهذا أقرب ما قيل، لكن لا يصلح للتعويل.
Seventh: It is celestial bodies different from it in its epicycle, not accepting illumination equally, maintaining their position with it always. And this is the closest of what has been said, but it is not suitable for reliance.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh opinion and an evaluation. The opinion suggests that the spots are actually other celestial bodies within the moon's epicycle, which do not receive light uniformly and are always in fixed positions relative to the moon. The author states that this is the most plausible opinion among those mentioned, but he still deems it insufficient for certainty. This shows the author's critical approach; he does not accept any of these opinions as definitively proven.

A) It is false.

D) It is the worst opinion.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi considers the seventh opinion the most plausible but refuses to rely on it due to lack of conclusive evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Amidi rejects even the most plausible opinion because it lacks certainty. Examine your own life: In what areas do you rely on 'closest' but unproven assumptions about your faith? Are you content with likelihoods or do you seek certainty? What would it take for you to stop relying on an opinion that is merely 'closest'?
?
The seventh opinion was the best available, yet Al-Amidi did not accept it. Think of a belief you hold because it 'makes sense' or is 'the best explanation among alternatives'. How would you feel if you had to reject it due to insufficient evidence? Could you live with uncertainty? Ask yourself: Am I willing to say 'I don't know' when no proof exists?
?
The author's refusal to rely on an unproven opinion shows intellectual honesty. Where in your life are you holding on to a belief or practice that has no solid proof from revelation or reason? What would it cost you to let it go? This lesson calls you to examine your own 'closest opinions' and test them against certainty.

Three Opinions on the Milky Way
Al-Amidi presents three opinions about the nature of the Milky Way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three historical naturalistic explanations for the Milky Way as presented by al-Amidi, including an objection to one of them.

Core Idea
Ancient scholars debated whether the Milky Way is fire, vapor, or a cluster of stars.

In this passage, al-Amidi presents three naturalistic opinions about the nature of the Milky Way that were current among philosophers. These explanations attempted to account for the appearance of the Milky Way without reference to divine intervention. One of them is accompanied by a brief refutation, setting the stage for al-Amidi's own evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the three opinions. Note the structure: the first two opinions are introduced with 'قيل' and the third with 'وقيل'.
Pay attention to the objection attached to the second opinion.
2
Identify each opinion
List the three opinions: 1) a burning from the sun in past times, 2) a smoky vapor in the air, 3) a cluster of small stars. Understand the key terms for each.
The first opinion requires the sun to have heat and the sphere to be burnable.
3
Analyze the refutation
Notice that only the second opinion (smoky vapor) is refuted. The objection says if it were vapor, its appearance would vary with seasons, which it does not.
This shows that al-Amidi is not just listing but critically engaging.
4
Contrast the third opinion
The third opinion explains the Milky Way as many tiny stars appearing as a cloud. Consider how this explanation avoids the seasonal objection.
This view aligns with modern understanding of the Milky Way as a galaxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المجرة وهي الدائرة التبنية المسماة عند العوام بسبيل التبانين . قيل: احتراق حدث من الشمس في تلك الدائرة في بعض الأزمان السالفة وإنما يصح إذا كان الشمس موصوفة بالحرارة والإحراق وكان الفلك قابلا للتأثر والاحتراق . وقيل : بخار دخاني واقع في الهواء ويرد عليه أنه يلزم منه اختلافها في الصيف والشتاء لقلة المدد في أحدهما وكثرته في الآخر. وقيل : كواكب صغار مقاربة متشابكة لا تتمايز حساً بل هي لشدة تكاثفها وصغرها صارت كأنها لطخات سحابية.
Concerning the Milky Way, which is the straw circle called by the common people the 'Way of the Straw-sellers.' It was said: a burning that occurred from the sun in that circle in some past times, and this is only valid if the sun is described with heat and burning and the sphere is susceptible to affect and burning. And it was said: a smoky vapor occurring in the air, and it is objected to it that it would necessitate its variation in summer and winter due to the scarcity of the substance in one and its abundance in the other. And it was said: small stars, close together, resembling each other, not distinguishable by sense, but due to their density and smallness they become as if they were cloudy patches.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents three opinions about the Milky Way. The first opinion claims it is a remnant of an ancient solar fire that once burned in that circle, requiring the sun to have heat and the sphere to be burnable. The second opinion says it is a smoky vapor in the air; the objection notes it would vary with seasons due to differences in vapor supply. The third opinion holds it is a dense cluster of tiny stars too close to distinguish individually, appearing as a cloud. The passage is purely expository; refutations are given only for the second opinion. This sets up al-Amidi's critique in the next lesson.

Key Terms
المجرة
الدائرة التبنية
سبيل التبانين
احتراق
بخار دخاني
كواكب صغار
لطخات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Milky Way has a single obvious natural explanation.
Correction: The passage shows multiple competing naturalistic explanations were proposed, indicating the phenomenon was debated.

Checkpoint
Which opinion about the Milky Way is accompanied by a refutation in this passage?
A) The ancient solar fire.
B) The smoky vapor.
C) The cluster of small stars.
D) None of them.

Key Takeaway
The Milky Way was explained by ancient philosophers as either an ancient solar fire, a smoky vapor, or a dense cluster of small stars, with the vapor theory being subject to a seasonal objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection to the second opinion is that it would lead to variation, yet the Milky Way appears constant. Are there beliefs you hold that would 'vary' with circumstances if tested? How can you seek consistency in your faith?

Definitions of the Three Souls: Plant, Animal, Human
The author defines the three souls with their respective restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three souls (plant, animal, human) and how the restrictions in each definition distinguish them.

Core Idea
Each soul is defined by a specific respect, not absolutely.

In this passage, the author presents the standard philosophical definitions of the three types of souls. He begins by clarifying that the plant soul is not a perfection in every respect, but only in respect of nutrition and growth, thus excluding the other souls. Then he defines the animal and human souls with their characteristic functions. This sets the foundation for understanding the hierarchy of living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the structure: first a clarification on the plant soul definition, then the definitions of animal and human souls.
The passage uses technical terms like 'كمال أول' and 'جسم طبيعي آلي'.
2
Understand the restriction in the plant soul definition
The restriction 'من حيث يتغذى وينمو' limits the plant soul's perfection to the respect of nutrition and growth. This excludes animal and human souls.
This shows that each soul is defined by a specific function.
3
Identify the definitions of animal and human souls
The animal soul is defined by sensation and voluntary movement; the human soul adds cognition of universals and deduction.
Note the progression from basic to higher functions.
4
Review the benefits of the restrictions
The restrictions ensure that each definition applies only to its respective soul and not others.
This is essential for accurate classification.

(Second:) The animal soul is a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of its sensing and moving voluntarily. And the benefits of the restrictions in these two definitions have appeared from what has passed.

This passage defines the three souls. It first explains the restriction in the definition of the plant soul, which is not an absolute perfection but only from the respect of nutrition and growth, thereby excluding the animal and human souls. Then it gives the definitions of the animal soul (sensing and voluntary movement) and the human soul (cognizing universals and deduction). The benefits of the restrictions are said to be clear from earlier discussion.

?

Proof of Non-Corporeal Principles from Diversity of Effects
Sanusi provides a proof from observed diversity of effects to non-corporeal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation that different effects arise from bodies sharing the same corporeality proves that the principles of those effects are non-corporeal powers.

Core Idea
Shared corporeality cannot explain diverse effects, so principles must be non-corporeal.

Sanusi begins with a simple observation: bodies produce different effects—sensation, movement, growth. Since all bodies are alike in being corporeal, the differences cannot come from corporeality alone. This leads to the conclusion that each body must have non-corporeal principles (powers) that are not bodies themselves. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: observation → contradiction → conclusion.
2
Understand the key inference
The argument hinges on the fact that effects are absent from other bodies that share the same corporeality. This shows the principles are not the shared corporeality.
Ask yourself: what is the shared property that cannot account for the differences?
3
Identify the nature of the principles
The text concludes they are 'powers attached to bodies' (قوى متعلقة بالأجسام) and not bodies themselves.
Why can't they be bodies? Because then the same question would recur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات) على فوائد يتحقق بها المرام في هذا المقام . ( الأول أنا نشاهد يصدر عنها آثار لا على نهج واحد كما ذكرنا من الحس والحركة والتغذي والنمو وتوليد المثل ( وليس ذلك الصدور عنها ( للجسمية المشتركة بين الأجسام كلها ( للتخلف ) أي تخلف تلك الآثار عن الأجسام الأخر المشاركة إياها في الجسمية ( فهي) أي تلك الآثار (لمبادئ ) في تلك الأجسام ( غير جسميتها ) وليست هذه المبادئ أجساماً. وإلا عاد الكلام فيها بل هي قوى متعلقة بالأجسام. نفساً ( وتسمى فالنفس لها اعتبارات ثلاثة وأسماء بحسبها فإنها (من حيث هي مبدأ الآثار المذكورة (قوة وبالقياس إلى المادة التي تحملها صورة و ) بالقياس (إلى طبيعة الجنس التي بها يتحصل ويتكمل ( كمال وتعريفها) أي تعريف النفس ( بالكمال أولى من الصورة إذ هي ( أي الصورة هي ( المنطبقة ( الحالة ( في المادة و ) النفس ( الناطقة ليست كذلك ) لأنها مجردة فلا يتناولها اسم الصورة إلا مجازاً من حيث إنها متعلقة بالبدن ويقوم به إمكانها قبل وجودها ( لكنها مع تجردها في ذاتها. قوله : ( يصدر عنها آثار إلخ الظاهر أن هذا الكلام يشعر أن جميع تلك الآثار صادرة عن تلك الأجسام. وكذا قوله : فهي لمبادئ في تلك الأجسام مشعر بظاهره أن جميع تلك الآثار لها مبادئ في الأجسام المذكورة مع أن بعض الآثار كالإدراكات العقلية إنما تصدر عندهم من المبدأ الفياض فيكون هو مبدأ لها. ويمكن أن يقال لعل المراد بمصدرية الشيء ومبدئية لها ما هو أعم من كونه فاعلا لها أو محلا لها وظاهر أن النفس الإنسانية محل لإدراكاتهما وإن لم تكن فاعلة لها، أو يقال : إن النفس الإنسانية بحركاتها الاختيارية في المعقولات تكون منشأ لاستعدادات موجبة لتلك الإدراكات فبهذا الاعتبار تجعل هي مبدأ لها .
Warnings containing benefits by which the aim is realized in this place. The first: We observe that [the body] produces effects not in a single manner, as we mentioned of sensation, movement, nutrition, growth, and reproduction. And that production is not due to the corporeality shared by all bodies, because [these effects] are absent from other bodies that share in corporeality. So those effects have principles in those bodies other than their corporeality, and these principles are not bodies; otherwise the discussion would recur. Rather, they are powers attached to bodies. And they are called soul. So the soul has three considerations and names accordingly: in terms of being the principle of the mentioned effects, it is power; in relation to the matter that bears it, it is form; and in relation to the nature of the genus through which it is realized and perfected, it is perfection. And defining the soul by perfection is better than by form, because form is what indwells in matter, and the rational soul is not like that, since it is abstract, so the name 'form' does not apply to it except metaphorically, inasmuch as it is attached to the body and its potentiality subsists through it before its existence, but with its abstraction in its essence. His statement: 'it produces effects, etc.' The apparent meaning of this statement is that all those effects proceed from those bodies. Similarly, his statement: 'they have principles in those bodies' apparently indicates that all those effects have principles in the mentioned bodies, even though some effects, like intellectual perceptions, according to them, proceed from the Emanating Principle (al-mabda' al-fayyad), so it is their principle. And it can be said: perhaps what is meant by something being a source or principle of something else is more general than its being an agent or a locus for it, and it is apparent that the human soul is a locus for its perceptions even if it is not an agent of them. Or it can be said: the human soul, through its voluntary movements in intelligibles, becomes an origin of dispositions necessitating those perceptions, so in this consideration it is made a principle for them.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) that provides a proof for the existence of non-corporeal principles (the soul) based on the diversity of effects. It argues that since different bodies share corporeality but produce different effects, the principles must be non-corporeal powers. Then it describes three aspects of the soul: as power (in relation to effects), as form (in relation to matter), and as perfection (in relation to genus). It explains why defining soul as perfection is better than as form, since the rational soul is abstract and cannot be called form literally. Then it raises an objection: the apparent wording suggests that all effects proceed from the body, but some effects (intellectual perceptions) come from an external principle (the Active Intellect). Two answers are given: either the term 'principle' is broader and includes being a locus, or the soul through its voluntary movements prepares dispositions that necessitate those perceptions, making it a principle indirectly.

Key Terms
آثار (effects)
الجسمية (corporeality)
مبادئ (principles)
قوة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the diversity of effects is due to the body's material composition itself.
Correction: The text argues that since all bodies share the same corporeality, the differences must come from non-corporeal principles, not from matter alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the diversity of effects be due to corporeality?
A) Corporeality is an abstract concept
B) Because all bodies share the same corporeality, so if effects were due to it, all bodies would produce the same effects
C) Because corporeality is not real
D) Because effects are illusions

Key Takeaway
The principle behind diverse effects must be non-corporeal because shared corporeality cannot account for differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is the non-corporeal principle that produces your actions, what does your daily conduct reveal about the state of your soul? Will you examine one action today and ask whether it springs from your soul or from mere bodily habit?
?
The proof shows that shared corporeality cannot account for your unique faculties like understanding and choice. What does that imply about your responsibility to use those faculties in a way that honors their non-corporeal origin?
?
You observe that some bodies lack the effects you possess—sensation, reason, will. How should that awareness shape your gratitude for the soul God has given you, and how can you actively nourish it beyond physical concerns?
