Three Aspects of the Soul and Why 'Perfection' is the Best Definition
Sanusi enumerates three aspects of the soul and argues for 'perfection' as the best definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways the soul is considered—as power, as form, and as perfection—and why defining it as perfection is superior to defining it as form.

Core Idea
The soul is power, form, and perfection, but only 'perfection' applies to the rational soul without metaphor.

Having established that the soul is a non-corporeal power, Sanusi now gives it a more precise analysis. He shows that the soul can be viewed in three relationships: as a power producing effects, as a form giving structure to matter, and as a perfection completing a genus. He then argues that 'perfection' is the most accurate definition, especially for the rational soul, which is abstract and cannot literally be a form inhering in matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations
Read the passage and note the three perspectives: principle of effects (power), relation to matter (form), relation to genus (perfection).
Each perspective corresponds to a different relationship of the soul.
2
Understand why 'perfection' is preferred
The text says form 'indwells' in matter, but the rational soul is abstract. So 'form' is only metaphorical for the rational soul, while 'perfection' is literal.
3
Reflect on the implication
This distinction shows that the human soul is not a material form but a separate abstract substance that nevertheless relates to the body.
Contrast with the plant and animal souls, which may be forms.

Resolving the Objection: Are All Effects from the Body's Own Principles?
Sanusi raises and resolves an objection about the origin of intellectual perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to resolve the objection that some effects (intellectual perceptions) come from an external source (the Emanating Principle) while the text implies all effects originate from the body's own principles.

Core Idea
The soul can be the principle of its perceptions either as a locus or as a preparer, even if the ultimate source is external.

The previous lesson's proof seemed to claim that all effects proceed from principles within the body. But what about intellectual perceptions, which many philosophers attribute to the Emanating Principle (al-mabda' al-fayyad)? Sanusi anticipates this objection and provides two answers: either 'principle' is used in a broad sense that includes being a locus, or the soul through its voluntary movements creates dispositions that necessitate perceptions, making it a principle in a secondary sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage where the objector points out that intellectual perceptions come from an external source, not from the body's own principles.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer broadens the meaning of 'principle' to include being a locus (محل) for the effect.
In this sense, the soul is a receptacle for perceptions, even if the active agent is external.
3
Understand the second answer
The second answer attributes a causal role to the soul: its voluntary movements in intelligibles generate dispositions that necessitate the perceptions, so the soul is a principle indirectly.
This preserves the idea that the soul is genuinely involved in producing intellectual perceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a note (tanbih) that provides a proof for the existence of non-corporeal principles (the soul) based on the diversity of effects. It argues that since different bodies share corporeality but produce different effects, the principles must be non-corporeal powers. Then it describes three aspects of the soul: as power (in relation to effects), as form (in relation to matter), and as perfection (in relation to genus). It explains why defining soul as perfection is better than as form, since the rational soul is abstract and cannot be called form literally. Then it raises an objection: the apparent wording suggests that all effects proceed from the body, but some effects (intellectual perceptions) come from an external principle (the Active Intellect). Two answers are given: either the term 'principle' is broader and includes being a locus, or the soul through its voluntary movements prepares dispositions that necessitate those perceptions, making it a principle indirectly.

Key Terms
المبدأ الفياض (Emanating Principle)
محل (locus)
استعدادات (dispositions)
فاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul has no role in intellectual perceptions because they come from an external source.
Correction: The two answers show that the soul is either the locus or a preparer, so it still has a role.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the passage, how does the human soul become a principle for intellectual perceptions?
A) By directly creating them
B) By being a passive recipient
C) By generating dispositions through voluntary movements
D) By merging with the Emanating Principle

Key Takeaway
Even if intellectual perceptions ultimately come from the Emanating Principle, the soul is either their locus or a preparer through its own activity, so the earlier claim that effects have principles in the body is preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you grasp a new truth about Allah or His creation, do you attribute that understanding solely to your own effort, or do you recognize it as a gift from the Emanating Principle? How might this shift your humility before knowledge?
?
The soul becomes a locus or preparer through its 'voluntary movements in intelligibles'. What deliberate actions are you taking to prepare your soul for deeper understanding of your deen, and where might you be passively expecting knowledge to appear?
?
If intellectual perceptions ultimately come from outside you, how should that affect your pride in your own intelligence? Will you let this lesson cultivate in you a posture of grateful receptivity rather than self-congratulation?

The Faculty of Hearing and Auditory Perception
Al-Iji defines hearing and its mechanism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Islamic definition of hearing and the mechanism of auditory perception as described by Al-Iji.

Core Idea
Hearing is the arrival of compressed air to the eardrum, not just a passive reception.

This lesson introduces the second faculty of the animal soul: hearing (sam'). Al-Iji defines hearing as the auditory power and explains that auditory perception occurs when compressed air between a striking and struck object reaches the eardrum. This brief definition grounds the classical theory of sound in physical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage is very brief but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the definition of hearing
Hearing is defined as 'the auditory faculty' (القوة السامعة). Recognize that this is a power of the animal soul.
3
Understand the mechanism of perception
Auditory perception requires compressed air (الهواء المنضغط) between the striker (القارع) and the struck (المقروع) to reach the eardrum (الصماخ).
The compression provides the force for perception.
4
Reflect on the condition
Note that hearing is not simply the ear receiving sound; it depends on a specific physical process. This was the standard view in classical physics and theology.
This connects to the broader natural philosophy of the time.

The second indicator: Hearing, i.e., the auditory faculty. And auditory perception occurs, as previously mentioned, by the arrival of the air compressed between the striker and the struck to the eardrum, by a strength obtained.

The phrase 'by a strength obtained' suggests that this compression provides the force necessary for perception. This is a brief definition, consistent with the classical theory of sound as a vibration transmitted through the air to the ear.

Perception by Essence and Accident: The Relational Meaning
The author explains the meaning of 'visible by essence and accident' and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between perception by essence and by accident, and how the same perception can relate to two objects relationally.

Core Idea
Perception by accident is not a second perception but a relational attribution of one perception.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies a subtle point in sensory perception: when we say something is perceived 'by accident,' it does not mean a separate act of perception, but rather that the same perception that perceives one thing also perceives another through it. The author uses the analogy of whiteness subsisting in a surface and body to illustrate relational attribution, then applies it to vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the analogy of whiteness used at the beginning.
2
Understand the analogy of whiteness
The author uses whiteness subsisting in a surface (by essence) and in a body (by accident) to show that there is only one subsistence, attributed relationally.
This analogy sets up the pattern for vision.
3
Apply to visibility
Color is visible by essence because vision relates to it directly, even if it depends on light. Magnitude is visible by accident because the same vision that sees color also sees magnitude.
Contrast with the view that would require two separate visions.
4
Note the refutation and conclusion
The author refutes the Imam's objection by showing a necessary distinction between this relational perception and abstract relations like paternity. He concludes that the term 'perceptible by accident' is better applied to this relational meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن البياض مثلاً قائم بالسطح أولاً وبالذات وقائم بالجسم ثانياً وبالعرض، ولا شبهة في أنه ليس معنى ذلك أن للبياض قيامين أحدهما بالسطح والآخر بالجسم، بل معناه أن له قياما واحداً بالسطح، لكن لما قام السطح بالجسم صار ذلك القيام منسوباً إلى السطح أولاً وبالذات، وإلى الجسم ثانياً وبالعرض فقس على ذلك معنى كون الشيء مثلاً مرئياً بالذات والعرض. فإذا قلنا : اللون مرئي بالذات كان معناه أن الرؤية متعلقة به بلا توسط تعلق تلك الرؤية بغيره وذلك لا ينافي كون رؤيته مشروطة برؤية أخرى متعلقة بالضوء فيكون كلاً منها مرئيين بالذات، لكن رؤية أحدهما مشروطة برؤية الآخر. وإذا قلنا : المقدار مرئي بالعرض بواسطة اللون كان معناه أن هناك رؤية واحدة متعلقة باللون أولاً وبالذات والمقدار ثانياً وبالعرض وهكذا الحال في سائر الأمور التي سماها مشتركة بين الحواس فهي محسوسة تبعاً قطعاً، وأما كون الشخص أبا عمرو فلا تعلق للإحساس به البتة والمنصف إذا رجع إلى نفسه وجد تفرقة ضرورية بينهما، وعلم أن المقدار مثلاً له انكشاف في الحس ليس ذلك الانكشاف للأبوة فاتضح الفرق بين معنى المحسوس بالعرض، واندفع ما ذكره الإمام بل نقول : إطلاق هذا الاسم على المعنى الأول أولى كما أشار إليه المصنف بإيراد كلمة قد في المعنى الآخر.
For example, whiteness subsists in the surface first and by essence, and subsists in the body secondarily and by accident. There is no doubt that this does not mean that whiteness has two subsistences, one in the surface and another in the body, but rather its meaning is that it has one subsistence in the surface. However, when the surface subsists in the body, that subsistence becomes attributed to the surface first and by essence, and to the body secondarily and by accident. So analogize to that the meaning of a thing being, for example, visible by essence and by accident. So if we say: color is visible by essence, its meaning is that vision is related to it without the intermediation of that vision being related to something else. And that does not contradict its vision being conditional upon another vision related to light, so each of them is visible by essence, but the vision of one is conditional upon the vision of the other. And if we say: magnitude is visible by accident through color, its meaning is that there is one vision related to color first and by essence and to magnitude secondarily and by accident. And such is the case in all matters that he called common between the senses: they are perceptible by way of following necessarily. As for a person being the father of Amr, perception has no relation to it at all. The fair-minded person, if he reflects within himself, finds a necessary distinction between them, and knows that magnitude, for example, has a disclosure in the sense that that disclosure is not for paternity. So the difference between the meaning of 'perceptible by accident' becomes clear, and what the Imam mentioned is refuted. Rather, we say: applying this name to the first meaning is more appropriate, as the author pointed out by using the word 'qad' in the other meaning.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between perception by essence and by accident. The author begins with an analogy: whiteness subsists in the surface essentially, but secondarily in the body because the surface subsists in the body. There are not two subsistences; rather, one subsistence is attributed relationally. Similarly, color is visible by essence because vision relates to it directly, even if that vision depends on light being seen first. Magnitude is visible by accident because the same vision that sees the color also sees the magnitude, but only through the color. The author then extends this to other common sensibles: they are perceived by following. He contrasts this with abstract relations like paternity, which are not perceived at all. He concludes that the distinction is necessary and that the term 'perceptible by accident' is better applied to the relational meaning, as the author (al-Iji) indicated.

Key Terms
visible by essence (مرئي بالذات)
visible by accident (مرئي بالعرض)
perceptible by accident (محسوس بالعرض)
subsistence (قيام)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'visible by accident' involves a separate act of vision.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the same vision attributed relationally, not a second vision.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of something being 'visible by accident'?
A) It has two separate acts of vision.
B) It is seen directly without mediation.
C) The same vision that sees another thing also sees it relationally.
D) It is not actually perceived at all.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between perception by essence and accident lies in relational attribution, not multiple acts of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that a person being a father is not perceptible by the senses, yet you often judge people by appearances. How can the distinction between what is directly and indirectly perceived help you avoid superficial judgments today? What specific step will you take to see beyond accidents?
?
When you look at the world, do you realize that most of what you see is perceived 'by accident' through color, shape, and light? Does this lead you to thank Allah for the gift of the senses or to feel humbled by how little you perceive directly? Commit to one moment today where you consciously reflect on the mediation of your perception.
?
The passage says that the fair-minded person finds a necessary distinction between perceptible and imperceptible things within himself. Have you ever reflected on your own intuitive distinction between what you truly perceive and what you merely infer? How can you use this awareness to strengthen your faith in the unseen realities that are not perceptible at all?

Definition of Common Sense Faculty
The author defines the common sense and its role in internal perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the common sense faculty as the internal receptacle of sensory forms and its role in perception.

Core Idea
Common sense is the inner gathering point for all sensory impressions.

Before examining the proofs for the common sense, we must first understand what it is. The author defines it as the internal faculty in which forms from the five external senses are imprinted. This lesson establishes that definition and explains why it is called a perceptive faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Notice how the author categorizes common sense as the first of five internal perceptive faculties.
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms 'common sense', 'internal perceptive faculty', 'sensory forms', and 'external senses' in the passage. Understand their meanings.
3
Understand the function
The external senses act as 'spies' gathering data; common sense receives this data; the soul then perceives through it. Reflect on this process.
Notice the analogy: the external senses are like scouts.

The second type: the internal perceptive faculty. That is, the faculties by which internal perception is completed, whether they are perceptive or assisting in perception. And they are also five: the first: the common sense, and it is the faculty in which the forms of particular things perceived by the five external senses are imprinted, which the spies have, so the soul examines them from there and perceives them. And since these faculties are an instrument for the soul in its perception, they are called perceptive for it. And it is established, i.e., it indicates the existence of the common sense, by three aspects: The first: Were it not that in us there is one faculty perceiving all sensible things such that they are imprinted in their entirety in it, we would not be able to judge some sensible things in relation to others affirmatively or negatively, such as judging that this touched thing is this colored thing, or that this colored thing is not that. For the judge, i.e., the one who makes a relative judgment, must have both parties present, i.e., the judged and that by which it is judged, so that he can observe the relation between them and affirm one of its two sides. And none of the external faculties is like that, so there must be an internal faculty. If it is said: The judge is the intellect, so there is no need for another faculty. We say: We will show that particulars are only perceived by physical faculties, so the intellect does not perceive them, so it does not judge upon them. Rather, there must be a physical faculty that perceives them entirely and judges between them. And one may say: What do you say about our judgment that Zayd is a human? If the perceiver of both is one, then the perceiver of the particular is the perceiver of the universal, namely the intellect, since the physical faculties cannot perceive universals. Then it would be possible that the judge between sensory particulars is the intellect. Otherwise, i.e., if their perceiver is not one, the basis of the proof is invalidated, namely that the judge must have both parties present. If it is said: The judge is the intellect as you indicated first, but the imprinting of sensory forms in it is impossible, so there must be a physical faculty in which all their forms are imprinted so that their presence before it can be conceived. The answer: Presence before the intellect does not necessarily require their gathering in one faculty; rather, perhaps the imprinting of them in multiple instruments of the intellect, like the external senses, suffices. The aspect: his saying 'whether perceptive' means as some hold. And his saying 'or assisting' means as others hold. And his saying 'like the spies': it is said 'jasast al-akhbar' and 'tajassastaha' meaning I investigated them from the spy, and it is narrated from al-Khalil 'al-jawwas'. His saying: 'like the external senses' - If it is said: The imprinting of sensory forms being sufficient for presence before the intellect is only when the material object is present at those external senses. But here it is assumed that the material object is absent from those senses; then it is not conceivable that that imprinting would be sufficient for presence before
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of internal perceptive faculty, specifically the common sense. The author states that there are five internal faculties, the first being common sense, which receives forms from the five external senses. He then gives three proofs for its existence; here he presents the first proof: We must have a single faculty that perceives all sensible forms together because we make judgments comparing different senses (e.g., 'this touched object is this seen color'). Such judgment requires the simultaneous presence of both items. External senses cannot do this because each sense only perceives its own object. Therefore, there must be an internal faculty that gathers all sensory forms. An objection is raised: The intellect could be the judge, as it is the faculty that makes judgments. The author replies that particulars are perceived only by physical faculties, so the intellect cannot directly judge them. Another objection: If the intellect perceives the particular and the universal in judgment like 'Zayd is human', then the intellect could be the judge over particulars. The response is that the intellect judges universals through concepts, but the judgment about particular sensory matters requires the sensory data to be present. The author then discusses a further objection about how forms can be present to the intellect if the material object is absent, but the response is that the intellect can use multiple sensory instruments. The passage ends with a gloss on terms used in the definition.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
القوة المدركة الباطنة
صور الجزئيات المحسوسة
الحواس الظاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the common sense according to the passage?
A) An external sense that perceives colors
B) An internal faculty in which forms from the five senses are imprinted
C) The intellect that judges sensory data
D) A faculty that controls movement

Key Takeaway
Common sense is the internal faculty that receives and holds sensory forms from all five external senses, making them available for the soul's perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Take a minute to mentally sift through the sensory images of this past hour — how many of them were beneficial for your soul, and how many were distractions? Commit to consciously guiding your senses toward what is wholesome.
?

Today, before you look, listen, or taste, pause and ask: 'Will this form ennoble my soul or distract it?' Let that question shape your choices.
?
If your common sense is filled with forms from heedless indulgence, your soul will be busy with them. Are you filling your inner canvas with images that draw you closer to Allah or farther away? Identify one source of sensory input that numbs your spiritual awareness and resolve to reduce it this week, replacing it with something that reminds you of your Creator.

First Proof for Common Sense: Argument from Comparative Judgment
The author presents the first of three proofs for the existence of the common sense faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the existence of the common sense faculty, which is based on our ability to compare and judge between different sensory inputs.

Core Idea
We must have a single faculty to combine senses, or comparison would be impossible.

Having defined common sense, the author now proves its existence with three arguments. The first argument is that we can make judgments that involve more than one sense, like saying 'this touched object is the same as this seen object'. Such a judgment requires the simultaneous presence of both sensory forms in a single perceiver. External senses operate separately, so there must be an internal faculty that gathers them together.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof premise
Read the initial argument: Without a single faculty that perceives all sensibles, cross-modal judgments would be impossible. Note the example of identifying a touched object as the same as a colored object.
2
Follow the first objection and response
The objector suggests the intellect could be the judge. The author responds that the intellect does not perceive sensory particulars; only physical faculties do.
3
Consider the second objection
The objector raises the judgment 'Zayd is human', which involves both a particular and a universal, arguing that the intellect might judge particulars.
4
Grasp the final response
The author answers that the intellect does not need all sensory forms in one faculty; it can use multiple external senses as instruments. The common sense still gathers the forms initially.
This response preserves the need for an internal faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second type: the internal perceptive faculty. That is, the faculties by which internal perception is completed, whether they are perceptive or assisting in perception. And they are also five: the first: the common sense, and it is the faculty in which the forms of particular things perceived by the five external senses are imprinted, which the spies have, so the soul examines them from there and perceives them. And since these faculties are an instrument for the soul in its perception, they are called perceptive for it. And it is established, i.e., it indicates the existence of the common sense, by three aspects: The first: Were it not that in us there is one faculty perceiving all sensible things such that they are imprinted in their entirety in it, we would not be able to judge some sensible things in relation to others affirmatively or negatively, such as judging that this touched thing is this colored thing, or that this colored thing is not that. For the judge, i.e., the one who makes a relative judgment, must have both parties present, i.e., the judged and that by which it is judged, so that he can observe the relation between them and affirm one of its two sides. And none of the external faculties is like that, so there must be an internal faculty. If it is said: The judge is the intellect, so there is no need for another faculty. We say: We will show that particulars are only perceived by physical faculties, so the intellect does not perceive them, so it does not judge upon them. Rather, there must be a physical faculty that perceives them entirely and judges between them. And one may say: What do you say about our judgment that Zayd is a human? If the perceiver of both is one, then the perceiver of the particular is the perceiver of the universal, namely the intellect, since the physical faculties cannot perceive universals. Then it would be possible that the judge between sensory particulars is the intellect. Otherwise, i.e., if their perceiver is not one, the basis of the proof is invalidated, namely that the judge must have both parties present. If it is said: The judge is the intellect as you indicated first, but the imprinting of sensory forms in it is impossible, so there must be a physical faculty in which all their forms are imprinted so that their presence before it can be conceived. The answer: Presence before the intellect does not necessarily require their gathering in one faculty; rather, perhaps the imprinting of them in multiple instruments of the intellect, like the external senses, suffices. The aspect: his saying 'whether perceptive' means as some hold. And his saying 'or assisting' means as others hold. And his saying 'like the spies': it is said 'jasast al-akhbar' and 'tajassastaha' meaning I investigated them from the spy, and it is narrated from al-Khalil 'al-jawwas'. His saying: 'like the external senses' - If it is said: The imprinting of sensory forms being sufficient for presence before the intellect is only when the material object is present at those external senses. But here it is assumed that the material object is absent from those senses; then it is not conceivable that that imprinting would be sufficient for presence before
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of internal perceptive faculty, specifically the common sense. The author states that there are five internal faculties, the first being common sense, which receives forms from the five external senses. He then gives three proofs for its existence; here he presents the first proof: We must have a single faculty that perceives all sensible forms together because we make judgments comparing different senses (e.g., 'this touched object is this seen color'). Such judgment requires the simultaneous presence of both items. External senses cannot do this because each sense only perceives its own object. Therefore, there must be an internal faculty that gathers all sensory forms. An objection is raised: The intellect could be the judge, as it is the faculty that makes judgments. The author replies that particulars are perceived only by physical faculties, so the intellect cannot directly judge them. Another objection: If the intellect perceives the particular and the universal in judgment like 'Zayd is human', then the intellect could be the judge over particulars. The response is that the intellect judges universals through concepts, but the judgment about particular sensory matters requires the sensory data to be present. The author then discusses a further objection about how forms can be present to the intellect if the material object is absent, but the response is that the intellect can use multiple sensory instruments. The passage ends with a gloss on terms used in the definition.

ثلاثة أوجه
الحكم ببعض المحسوسات على بعضها
القاضي
الحواس الظاهرة
القوة الباطنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the intellect alone can directly compare sensory data from different senses.
Correction: The author shows that the intellect does not perceive sensory particulars; a physical faculty (common sense) must first gather them.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must there be a common sense faculty?
A) Because external senses can compare data
B) Because we can judge that a touched object is the same as a seen colored object
C) Because the intellect needs a single faculty to perceive universals
D) Because the soul cannot perceive without it

Key Takeaway
The first proof for common sense is that comparative judgments between different senses are only possible if there is a single internal faculty that collects all sensory forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you touch something and see it at the same time, do you ever pause to recognize the inner process that combines these sensations? How does this awareness affect your trust in your own perceptions?
?
The proof argues that the intellect alone cannot judge between sensory inputs. In which area of your life have you relied solely on rational analysis while ignoring the integrated experience of your senses?
?
If your common sense is what unifies your sensory experiences, then every moment of perception is a testimony to a deeper, unifying faculty within you. How does this realization call you to be more present and grateful for the simple act of sensing?

Why Celestial Bodies Lack Senses and Passions
States the claim and gives the reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale for why celestial souls are devoid of external senses, appetite, and anger.

Core Idea
Celestial forms are incorruptible, so no need for sensory or emotional faculties.

In this passage, the author states a clear doctrine: the spheres have no external senses, no appetite, and no anger. The reasoning is based on the purpose of these faculties: to preserve the body by attracting benefit and repelling harm. Since the celestial bodies' forms are incorruptible, they have no need for such faculties. Let's examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and reasoning
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the three items: external senses, appetite, anger. Then see the reason: they are for preserving form from corruption, but celestial forms don't corrupt.
The reasoning hinges on the concept of 'corruption' (fasād).
2
Understand the purpose of senses and passions
According to the author, senses and passions exist to attract benefit and repel harm, ultimately to preserve the body. Ask yourself: why do we need senses? For survival. The spheres don't need to survive eternally.
3
Appreciate why celestial forms are incorruptible
The text says celestial forms do not admit disruption (kharq), reconnection (ilti'ām), generation (kawn), or decay (fasād). This is the key property that makes preservation unnecessary.
This is a standard argument in Islamic cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ليس للأفلاك حس من الحواس الظاهرة ولا شهوة ولا غضب لأن الاحتياج إليهما لجلب النفع ودفع الضر، المقصود بهما حفظ الصورة عن الفساد وصورها الجسمية والنوعية لا تقبل ذلك لامتناع الخرق والالتئام والكون والفساد عليها والمقدمات المذكورة كلها ممنوعة إذ لا نسلم أن هذا القوى إنما خلقت لما ذكر، فإنه يجوز أن يكون خلقها لكونها كمالاً للجسم ولا نسلم أيضاً انحصار النفع والدفع في حفظ الصورة الفساد، ولئن سلم فلا نسلم أن صورة الفلك لا تقبل الفساد وما استدل به عليه مدخول، وفي الملخص أن لكلام ابن سينا اضطرب في الحواس الباطنة فحيث نفاها استدل عليه بأنها متعلقة بالحواس الظاهرة، لأن التخيل لحفظ صور المحسوسات، والتوهم لدرك أحوالها الجزئية، والتفكر للتصرف فيها، فإذا لم يوجد الأصل وجب يوجد التبع، ويرد على هذا الاستدلال أنا لا نسلم انحصار فائدتها في حفظ المحسوسات وأحوالها الجزئية والتصرف فيها إذ يجوز أن يكون فيها فوائد أخرى وإن سلم فلا نسلم أنه لا معطل في الوجود
Second: The spheres do not have sense from the external senses, nor appetite, nor anger, because the need for them is to attract benefit and repel harm, the purpose of which is to preserve the form from corruption. But their bodily and specific forms do not admit that because of the impossibility of disruption and reconnection and generation and corruption upon them. The mentioned premises are all denied, for we do not grant that these powers were created only for what was mentioned; rather, it is possible that they were created because they are perfections of the body. Nor do we grant that benefit and repulsion are confined to preserving the form from corruption. Even if granted, we do not grant that the form of the sphere is susceptible to corruption; what is adduced for it is flawed. In al-Mulakhkhas, it is said that Ibn Sina's statement about the internal senses is confused: when he denies them, he argues that they are related to the external senses, because imagination is for preserving forms of sensed things, estimation for perceiving their particular states, and reflection for disposing of them. So if the original is absent, the dependent must be absent. The objection to this argument is: we do not grant that their benefit is confined to preserving sensed things and their particular states and disposing of them; it is possible that they have other benefits. Even if granted, we do not grant that there is nothing idle in existence.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch (tafri') and its accompanying objections and responses. The author states that the spheres lack external senses, appetite (shahwa), and anger (ghaḍab). The reason given is that these faculties exist primarily to attract benefit and repel harm, both aimed at preserving the body from corruption. However, the celestial bodies' forms—both bodily and specific—are impervious to corruption, as they do not undergo tearing, mending, generation, or decay. The author then notes that all these premises are contested. First, it is possible that these powers were created not just for preservation but as perfections of the body. Second, benefit and harm are not limited to physical preservation. Third, even if they were, it is not granted that the celestial form is incorruptible. The passage also mentions a confusion in Ibn Sina's view on internal senses: he denies them to the spheres by arguing that they are dependent on external senses, but this is questioned because their benefits may extend beyond sensory preservation, and the argument that 'nothing is idle in existence' (lā mu'aṭṭil fī al-wujūd) is not accepted.

Key Terms
حس
شهوة
غضب
صورة جسمية
صورة نوعية
خرق والئتام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that any living being must have senses and emotions.
Correction: This passage shows that celestial bodies, being incorruptible, have no need for them.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument given for why the spheres lack appetite and anger?
A) They are not alive.
B) They have no need to attract benefit or repel harm because their forms are incorruptible.
C) They are made of a different element.
D) They are moved by angels.

Key Takeaway
Celestial bodies lack senses and passions because their forms are incorruptible and do not require preservation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have senses and passions that constantly drive you to seek benefit and avoid harm. If your ultimate purpose were not the preservation of this corruptible body, but the cultivation of an incorruptible soul, how would your daily priorities need to change? What one attachment to a worldly benefit or hatred of a worldly harm are you willing to release today?
?
The spheres lack appetite and anger because they are perfect. Your own appetites and anger often lead you to sin. When you feel the pull of shahwa or the surge of ghaḍab, will you recognize them as signs of your own deficiency and turn to the One who alone can purify your soul? Or will you continue to justify them as 'natural'?
?
If the spheres—mere created bodies—are too noble for emotions like anger and lust, what does it say about your own nature that you were given these faculties? Are you using them for their intended purpose: to preserve your faith and body? Or have they become ends in themselves, enslaving you to this world?

Objections and Ibn Sina's Confusion
Raises objections to the previous argument and criticizes Ibn Sina on internal senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the premise that celestial bodies lack senses and passions, and the confusion in Ibn Sina's treatment of internal senses.

Core Idea
Every premise of the argument is contested, and even Ibn Sina's own reasoning is flawed.

After presenting the main argument, the author immediately subjects it to critical scrutiny. Every premise is denied: the purpose of these faculties may not be limited to preservation, the celestial form may not be absolutely incorruptible, and there may be other benefits. Additionally, a confusion in Ibn Sina's discussion of internal senses is highlighted. Let's break down these objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three denials
The text says 'all premises are denied' and then lists three: 1) the powers may be perfections, 2) benefit/harm not limited to preservation, 3) celestial form may be corruptible. Find these in the Arabic.
The phrase 'ولا نسلم' is used to deny each premise.
2
Understand the Ibn Sina confusion
Ibn Sina denied internal senses to the spheres because they depend on external senses. The author reports this and then objects: their benefits may extend beyond sensory preservation, and 'nothing idle' is not accepted.
This shows the debate about whether celestial beings have cognitive faculties.
3
Reflect on the force of the objections
These objections are not necessarily conclusive, but they show that the positive argument is not airtight. Kalam often proceeds by challenging premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Even if granted, we do not grant that there is nothing idle in existence.

?

The Soul is Created: Two Views on Timing
The author states the agreed creation of the soul among monotheists and the dispute over its timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among monotheists on the soul's createdness and the two positions on when it is created (with the body or before).

Core Idea
All monotheists agree the soul is created, but they differ on whether it arises with the body or precedes it.

This lesson introduces the central question of the soul's origin. The author first states the universal agreement that the soul is not eternal, then presents the two competing views about its creation timing, each supported by scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the agreement on creation
Start by reading the sentence that states the consensus among monotheists (المليون) that the soul is created, since only God and His attributes are eternal.
The term 'المليون' refers to adherents of prophetic religions.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two opinions: one says the soul is created with the body, the other says it is created before. Note the proof each group uses.
The first group cites Quran 23:14; the second cites a hadith.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this question matters in Islamic theology—it relates to the soul's relationship with the body and its pre-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( في أن النفس الناطقة حادثة اتفق عليه المليون إذ لا قديم عندهم إلا الله وصفاته ) عند من أثبتها زائدة على ذاته ) لكنهم اختلفوا في أنها هل تحدث مع) حدوث ( البدن أو قبله فقال : بعضهم تحدث معه لقوله تعالى بعد تعداد أطوار البدن ثم أنشأناه خلقاً آخر ﴾ [المؤمنون: ٤١] ، والمراد بهذا الإنشاء (إفاضة النفس) على البدن ) وقال بعضهم : بل قبله لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : خلق الله الأرواح قبل الأجساد بألفي عام وغاية هذه الأدلة الظن ) دون اليقين الذي هو المطلوب ( أما الآية فلجواز أن يريد بقوله : ثم أنشأناه جعل النفس متعلقة به وإنما يلزم من ) حدوث تعلقها لا حدوث ذاتها وأما الحديث فلأنه خبر واحد فتعارضه مقطوعة المتن مظنونة الدلالة والحديث بالعكس فلكل رجحان من وجه فيتقاومان
(On the rational soul being created: the monotheists agreed upon it, for there is no eternal with them except God and His attributes) according to those who affirm them as additional to His essence. But they disagreed as to whether it occurs with the occurrence of the body or before it. Some said: it occurs with it, due to His saying, after counting the stages of the body, 'Then We produced it as another creation' [Quran 23:14], and what is meant by this production is the effusion of the soul upon the body. And some said: rather before it, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'God created the souls before the bodies by two thousand years.' But the utmost of these evidences is conjecture, not certainty which is required. As for the verse, it is possible that by His saying 'Then We produced it' He meant making the soul attached to it, and only the temporality of its attachment follows, not the temporality of its essence. And as for the hadith, it is a solitary report, so a text of certain transmission and conjectural indication opposes it, and the hadith is the opposite; so each has a preponderance from one aspect, so they counterbalance each other.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the creation of the rational soul. The author begins by stating that all adherents of prophetic religions (المليون) agree that the soul is created, since they hold that nothing is eternal except God and His attributes. However, they differ on whether the soul is created simultaneously with the body or prior to it. The author presents two positions: one group says it occurs with the body, citing the Quranic verse about creating a new creation after the bodily stages, interpreted as the effusion of the soul. Another group says it occurs before, citing a hadith that souls were created before bodies by two thousand years. The author then evaluates these proofs: both amount only to conjecture, not certainty. The Quranic verse could mean the attachment of the soul, not its essence, so it does not prove the soul's own creation. The hadith is a solitary report, whose text is of certain transmission but its indication is conjectural, while the verse is the opposite, so they balance each other without decisive proof. This shows the author's critical approach to scriptural evidence in speculative theology.

Evaluating the Evidence for the Soul's Origin
The author argues that the evidence for the soul's creation timing is merely conjectural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique that the Quranic verse and hadith only provide conjectural evidence for the soul's creation timing.

Core Idea
Both the Quranic verse and the hadith fall short of certainty, leaving the timing of the soul's creation unresolved.

After presenting the two positions, the author critically examines the proof texts. He shows that neither the verse nor the hadith provides decisive evidence, and he explains why they balance each other, resulting in conjecture rather than certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's evaluation
Start with the sentence 'وغاية هذه الأدلة الظن' to understand the overall verdict: the evidence is merely conjectural.
The author is a kalam scholar who demands certainty (اليقين) in theological matters.
2
Analyze the critique of the verse
Notice the author's point: the verse could refer to the attachment of the soul, not its creation, so it does not prove the soul's temporality.
This is a linguistic and interpretive nuance.
3
Analyze the critique of the hadith
The hadith is a solitary report (خبر واحد) with certain transmission but conjectural indication; it is countered by the verse which has opposite strengths.
This shows the author's method of weighing evidence.

Aristotle's Proof for the Creation of the Soul
Aristotle's reductio argument for the soul's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's reductio ad absurdum proof that the human soul cannot be pre-eternal, because assuming its eternity leads to either reincarnation or material division, both of which are impossible.

Core Idea
If the soul were eternal, it would lead to reincarnation or material division — both impossible.

This passage presents Aristotle's famous reductio ad absurdum proof that the human soul is created, not eternal. By examining the logical consequences of assuming the soul's pre-eternity, Aristotle shows that such an assumption collapses into contradictions. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the main dilemma
Aristotle begins by asking: if the soul were eternal, then before attachment to the body, are souls many and distinct or one? This is the branching point.
The argument is a dilemma: each branch leads to an absurdity.
3
Trace the first branch: souls are distinct
If souls are many and distinct before attachment, their distinction comes either from their essence or from something else. If from essence, each soul is a unique species (absurd). If from something else, it must be the body, leading to reincarnation (absurd).
Both sub-branches are impossible, so this branch fails.
4
Trace the second branch: souls are one
If before attachment souls are one, after attachment either they remain one (making all souls identical, absurd) or they become multiple (requiring division, making the soul material and causing the original eternal soul to perish, absurd).
This branch also fails, so the assumption of eternity is false.
5
Conclude the argument
Since both possibilities lead to absurdities, the original assumption (soul is eternal) must be false. Therefore, the soul is created.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا) كما ذكرناه (و) أما (الحكماء ) فإنهم قد اختلفوا في حدوثها فقال به أرسطو ومن تبعه، ومنعه من قبله وقالوا بقدمها احتج أرسطو بأنها لو قدمت فإما أن تكون قبل التعلق بالبدن متعددة (متمايزة أولا فإن كانت متمايزة فتمايزها) وتعينها (إما بذواتها أولا بذواتها فإن كان (بذواتها أو بلوازمها ( فتكون كل نفس) من النفوس البشرية ( نوعاً منحصراً في الشخص الواحد ( فيلزم اختلاف كل نفسين بالحقيقة . وإنه باطل إذ لو لم نقل بأن كلها متماثلة فلا أقل من أن يوجد ) فيما بين الجميع ( نفسان متماثلان وإن كان تمايزها ( لا بذواتها كان بالقابل وما يكتنفه كما تقدم) من أن تعدد أفراد النوع الواحد معلل بقابله والأعراض المكتنفة به ( ومادتها البدن فتكون متعلقة قبل هذا البدن ببدر آخر ويلزم التناسخ) أي انتقالها من بدن إلى آخر ) وسنبطله وإن لم تكن قبل التعلق ( متمايزة ) بل كانت واحدة ( فبعد التعلق إن بقيت ) على وحدتها ) كما كانت كانت نفس زيد هي بعينها نفس عمرو فيلزم أن يشتركا في صفات النفس من العلم والقدرة واللذة والألم وسائر الصفات وأنه باطل بالضرورة ) وإن لم تبق كما كانت ( بل تكثرت ( لزم التجزؤ والانقسام ولا يتصور إلا فيما له مقدار وحجم فلا تكون مجردة بل مادية ( وأيضاً فقد عدمت ) بذلك التجزؤ والانقسام ( تلك الهوية ( الواحدة القديمة ) وحصلت هويتان أخريان حادثتان ويلزم المطلوب) وهو أن النفوس المتعلقة بالأبدان حادثة
(This is as we mentioned). And as for the philosophers, they differed about its creation. Aristotle and those who followed him affirmed it, and those before him denied it and said it is eternal. Aristotle argued that if it were eternal, then either before attachment to the body it is multiple and distinct, or not. If it is distinct, then its distinction and individuation is either through its own essence or not. If through its own essence or its concomitants, then each human soul would be a species confined to one individual, necessitating that every two souls differ in reality. This is false, for if we do not say all are identical, then at least there exist two similar souls among them. If its distinction is not through its own essence, then it is through the receptacle and its attendant circumstances, as previously mentioned — that the multiplicity of individuals of one species is explained by the receptacle and attendant accidents — and its matter is the body, so it would have been attached before this body to another body, and reincarnation follows, i.e., its transmigration from one body to another, and we will refute it. And if before attachment it was not distinct but one, then after attachment, if it remains as it was in its unity, then Zayd's soul would be the very same as Amr's soul, necessitating that they share in the attributes of the soul — knowledge, power, pleasure, pain, and all attributes — and that is necessarily false. And if it does not remain as it was but becomes multiple, then division and partition follow, which is inconceivable except in something having magnitude and volume, so it would not be immaterial but material. Also, by that division and partition, that one eternal entity would cease to exist and two other created entities would come into existence, and the desired conclusion follows: that the souls attached to bodies are created.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's proof for the creation of the soul, as reported by the author. The philosophers (الحكماء) disagreed on the soul's origin: Aristotle and his followers said it is created, while earlier philosophers said it is eternal. Aristotle's argument is a dilemma: if the soul were pre-eternal, then before attachment to the body, either souls are many and distinct or they are one. If they are many and distinct, their distinction either comes from their essence or from something else. If from essence, each soul would be a unique species, meaning no two souls are alike—but we know souls can be similar. If from something else, then the cause is the body, leading to the soul having been attached to a previous body, implying reincarnation (التناسخ), which the author says will be refuted. If before attachment souls are one, then after attachment either they remain one—meaning Zayd's soul is Amr's soul, which is absurd—or they become multiple, which requires division, meaning the soul is material, not immaterial, and the original eternal soul perishes, giving rise to new created souls. Thus, the conclusion that souls are created is established. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Misconception: One might think Aristotle believed in the soul's pre-existence, but this passage shows he argued for its creation.
Correction: Aristotle argued that the soul cannot be pre-eternal; it must be created. The passage refutes the idea of pre-eternity by showing it leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's argument shows that the soul's creation is necessary because assuming its pre-eternity leads to logical absurdities such as reincarnation, identity of distinct souls, or material division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect: Are you living as if your soul is distinct and unrepeatable, or as if you could be replaced? What would change if you truly believed no one else can bear your responsibilities before Allah?
?
Aristotle proved your soul is created, not eternal. That means your existence began at Allah's command and will end at His will. Are you living in awareness of your soul's createdness, or do you act as if it will last forever without reckoning? What specific habit would you change today to align with this truth?

First Argument: The Soul Must Be Material
First argument and its refutation regarding matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the soul's pre-eternity and its refutation based on the concept of matter.

Core Idea
Origination implies matter, but not the kind of matter that precludes immateriality.

The first argument attempts to prove the soul's pre-eternity by claiming that if the soul were created, it would be material. The author responds by distinguishing between two types of matter, showing that the soul can be immaterial even if attached to matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, then 'قلنا' indicating the response.
2
Identify the opponent's argument.
The opponent says every originated thing has matter; so if the soul is originated, it must be material. Understand the logical form.
3
Understand the refutation.
The author concedes the implication but broadens the meaning of 'matter'. Focus on the distinction between matter of subsistence and matter of attachment.
The phrase 'أعم مادة يحل الحادث فيها أو يتعلق بها' is key.
4
Reflect on the significance.
Why does this distinction preserve the soul's immateriality? How does it allow the soul to be originated yet immaterial?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم) على قدمها ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة : الأول : إن كل حادث له مادة ) فلو كانت النفس حادثة كانت مادية لا مجردة ( قلنا : ( بعد تسليم الملازمة تلك المادة التي يستلزمها الحدوث ( أعم مادة يحل الحادث ( فيها أو يتعلق بها والمتعلق بالمادة يجوز أن يكون مجردا بحسب ذاته ( الثاني : لو لم تكن الناطقة ( أزلية لم تكن أبدية ) أيضاً والتالي باطل اتفاقاً وأما الملازمة فلأنها إذا كانت حادثة يزول وجودها لأن كل كائن فاسد . والجواب : المنع ومعنى القضية المذكورة أن كل حادث فهو في حد ذاته قابل للعدم وليس يلزم منه طريانه عليه لجواز أن يمتنع عدمه لغيره أبدا . ( الثالث : يلزم عدم تناهي (الأبدان والصواب عدم تناهي النفوس وذلك لأنها إذا كانت حادثة كان حدوثها بحدوث الأبدان التي هي شرط فيضانها من المبدأ القديم، والأبدان غير متناهية لا استنادها إلى اقتضاء الأدوار الفلكية التي لا تتناهى فتكون النفوس البشرية غير متناهية أيضاً لكن لا استحالة في لاتناهي الأبدان والأدوار لأنها متعاقبة بخلاف النفوس فإنها باقية بعد المفارقة فيلزم اجتماع أمور موجودة غير متناهية وهو محال بالتطبيق . ( والجواب شرط امتناعه الترتب ) الطبيعي أو الوضعي ( كما مر ) . والنفوس الناطقة وإن كانت موجودة مجتمعة إلا أنها غير مترتبة فيجوز لا تناهيها . (تنبيه : ) قال أرسطو : كل حادث لا بد له من استناده إلى المبدأ القديم الواجب ( من شرط (من حادث) فقوله: (دفعاً للدور والتسلسل تعليل لما هو المقدر في الكلام وأما من
The opponent argued for its pre-eternity with three arguments. The first: every originated thing has matter; so if the soul were originated, it would be material, not immaterial. We said: after conceding the implication, the matter that origination entails is more general: it is matter in which the originated thing subsists or to which it is attached, and that which is attached to matter may be immaterial in its essence. The second: if the rational soul were not eternal a parte ante, it would not be eternal a parte post either, and the consequent is false by consensus. As for the implication, it is because if it is originated, its existence perishes, since every arising thing is corrupt. The answer: deny it; the meaning of the mentioned proposition is that every originated thing is in itself susceptible to non-existence, but it does not necessarily entail that non-existence actually occurs to it, because it is possible that its non-existence is prevented by another forever. The third: it entails the non-finiteness of bodies, and the correct view is the non-finiteness of souls. This is because if they are originated, their origination occurs with the origination of bodies which are a condition for their emanation from the eternal principle; bodies are infinite due to their dependence on the requirements of celestial cycles which are infinite; so human souls are also infinite. However, there is no impossibility in the infinity of bodies and cycles because they are successive, unlike souls which remain after separation, so there would be a simultaneous existence of infinite things, which is impossible according to the principle of correspondence. The answer: the condition for its impossibility is natural or positional ordering, as has passed. And the rational souls, even though they exist simultaneously, are not ordered, so their infinity is permissible. (Note:) Aristotle said: every originated thing must depend on the eternal necessary principle through a condition from an originated thing; his saying: 'to avoid circularity and infinite regress' is an explanation of what is presumed in the discourse, and as for...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments made by opponents for the pre-eternity (قدم) of the soul, along with their refutations. The first argument: every originated thing has matter, so if the soul were originated, it would be material, not immaterial. The author responds by distinguishing the type of matter: matter can be that in which something inheres or to which it is attached; something attached to matter may still be immaterial in essence. The second argument: if the soul were not without beginning, it would not be without end, because everything that comes into being perishes. The author denies the implication: originated things are in themselves capable of non-existence, but they may be preserved perpetually by another (i.e., God), so the soul can be everlasting despite being created. The third argument: if souls are created, they arise with bodies, which are infinite due to infinite celestial cycles, so souls would be infinite in number. But bodies and cycles are successive, while souls persist after death, leading to an infinite number of coexisting souls, which is impossible by the principle of correspondence. The author responds that the impossibility requires orderly arrangement (natural or positional), whereas souls are not ordered, so infinity is permissible. Finally, a note (تنبيه) begins with Aristotle's statement about every originated needing a condition to depend on the eternal, but the text cuts off, making the note incomplete.

مادة
مجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being attached to matter necessarily makes a thing material.
Correction: The author shows that attachment to matter does not preclude immateriality of essence.

Checkpoint

A) Only matter of inherence (subsistence)
B) Only matter of attachment
C) Both matter of inherence and matter of attachment
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The soul can be originated yet immaterial because the matter it requires is not the matter of inherence but of attachment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is attached to your body but not material, how does that change the way you view your physical desires? Will you continue to let them dominate you, or will you train your soul to lead?
?
This lesson shows that being connected to matter does not make you material. Are you living as if your identity is tied to your body and possessions, or as an immaterial soul responsible to its Creator?
?
The opponent's argument assumed that origination implies materiality. What assumptions do you hold about yourself that might be false because you have not distinguished between attachment and essence?

Second Argument: Eternal a Parte Post Requires Eternal a Parte Ante
Second argument and its refutation regarding eternity a parte post.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that links the soul's beginninglessness with its endlessness and its refutation.

Core Idea
Being created does not prevent eternal preservation by another.

The second argument asserts that if the soul is not eternal without beginning, it cannot be eternal without end, because all originated things perish. The author refutes this by denying the implication: originated things are susceptible to non-existence, but their non-existence can be prevented by God's power, so the soul can be everlasting despite being created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the opponent's claim: 'لو لم تكن الناطقة أزلية لم تكن أبدية'.
2
Understand the opponent's logic.
The opponent argues that if the soul is not pre-eternal, it must be originated, and all originated things are corruptible, so it would not be everlasting.
3
Examine the author's response.
The author denies the implication. He explains that originated things are in themselves possible of non-existence, but they can be preserved forever by another (God).
The phrase 'لجواز أن يمتنع عدمه لغيره أبدا' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the conclusion.
The soul can be created yet everlasting because God perpetually sustains it. This avoids the contradiction.

احتج الخصم) على قدمها ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة : الأول : إن كل حادث له مادة ) فلو كانت النفس حادثة كانت مادية لا مجردة ( قلنا : ( بعد تسليم الملازمة تلك المادة التي يستلزمها الحدوث ( أعم مادة يحل الحادث ( فيها أو يتعلق بها والمتعلق بالمادة يجوز أن يكون مجردا بحسب ذاته ( الثاني : لو لم تكن الناطقة ( أزلية لم تكن أبدية ) أيضاً والتالي باطل اتفاقاً وأما الملازمة فلأنها إذا كانت حادثة يزول وجودها لأن كل كائن فاسد . والجواب : المنع ومعنى القضية المذكورة أن كل حادث فهو في حد ذاته قابل للعدم وليس يلزم منه طريانه عليه لجواز أن يمتنع عدمه لغيره أبدا . ( الثالث : يلزم عدم تناهي (الأبدان والصواب عدم تناهي النفوس وذلك لأنها إذا كانت حادثة كان حدوثها بحدوث الأبدان التي هي شرط فيضانها من المبدأ القديم، والأبدان غير متناهية لا استنادها إلى اقتضاء الأدوار الفلكية التي لا تتناهى فتكون النفوس البشرية غير متناهية أيضاً لكن لا استحالة في لاتناهي الأبدان والأدوار لأنها متعاقبة بخلاف النفوس فإنها باقية بعد المفارقة فيلزم اجتماع أمور موجودة غير متناهية وهو محال بالتطبيق . ( والجواب شرط امتناعه الترتب ) الطبيعي أو الوضعي ( كما مر ) . والنفوس الناطقة وإن كانت موجودة مجتمعة إلا أنها غير مترتبة فيجوز لا تناهيها . (تنبيه : ) قال أرسطو : كل حادث لا بد له من استناده إلى المبدأ القديم الواجب ( من شرط (من حادث) فقوله: (دفعاً للدور والتسلسل تعليل لما هو المقدر في الكلام وأما من
The opponent argued for its pre-eternity with three arguments. The first: every originated thing has matter; so if the soul were originated, it would be material, not immaterial. We said: after conceding the implication, the matter that origination entails is more general: it is matter in which the originated thing subsists or to which it is attached, and that which is attached to matter may be immaterial in its essence. The second: if the rational soul were not eternal a parte ante, it would not be eternal a parte post either, and the consequent is false by consensus. As for the implication, it is because if it is originated, its existence perishes, since every arising thing is corrupt. The answer: deny it; the meaning of the mentioned proposition is that every originated thing is in itself susceptible to non-existence, but it does not necessarily entail that non-existence actually occurs to it, because it is possible that its non-existence is prevented by another forever. The third: it entails the non-finiteness of bodies, and the correct view is the non-finiteness of souls. This is because if they are originated, their origination occurs with the origination of bodies which are a condition for their emanation from the eternal principle; bodies are infinite due to their dependence on the requirements of celestial cycles which are infinite; so human souls are also infinite. However, there is no impossibility in the infinity of bodies and cycles because they are successive, unlike souls which remain after separation, so there would be a simultaneous existence of infinite things, which is impossible according to the principle of correspondence. The answer: the condition for its impossibility is natural or positional ordering, as has passed. And the rational souls, even though they exist simultaneously, are not ordered, so their infinity is permissible. (Note:) Aristotle said: every originated thing must depend on the eternal necessary principle through a condition from an originated thing; his saying: 'to avoid circularity and infinite regress' is an explanation of what is presumed in the discourse, and as for...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments made by opponents for the pre-eternity (قدم) of the soul, along with their refutations. The first argument: every originated thing has matter, so if the soul were originated, it would be material, not immaterial. The author responds by distinguishing the type of matter: matter can be that in which something inheres or to which it is attached; something attached to matter may still be immaterial in essence. The second argument: if the soul were not without beginning, it would not be without end, because everything that comes into being perishes. The author denies the implication: originated things are in themselves capable of non-existence, but they may be preserved perpetually by another (i.e., God), so the soul can be everlasting despite being created. The third argument: if souls are created, they arise with bodies, which are infinite due to infinite celestial cycles, so souls would be infinite in number. But bodies and cycles are successive, while souls persist after death, leading to an infinite number of coexisting souls, which is impossible by the principle of correspondence. The author responds that the impossibility requires orderly arrangement (natural or positional), whereas souls are not ordered, so infinity is permissible. Finally, a note (تنبيه) begins with Aristotle's statement about every originated needing a condition to depend on the eternal, but the text cuts off, making the note incomplete.

Key Terms
أزلية
أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all originated things must eventually cease to exist.
Correction: The author shows that originated things can be preserved perpetually by an external power.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the argument that the soul's createdness would make it perishable?
A) He agrees that created things perish
B) He denies that created things are perishable
C) He says created things are in themselves possible of non-existence but can be preserved forever
D) He says the soul is not created

Key Takeaway
Createdness does not imply eventual annihilation; the soul is everlasting because God preserves it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with the quiet fear that your soul might eventually perish because of your faults, or do you truly trust that God's preservation is sufficient to sustain you forever? What would change in your daily life if you fully embraced that your everlastingness depends entirely on Him?
?
Your soul is inherently fragile and capable of ceasing to exist—yet God chooses to preserve it. In what area of your life have you been acting as if your own strength or goodness is what keeps you spiritually alive, rather than His constant, undeserved preservation?
?
If God's prevention of annihilation is the only reason you will exist forever, how does that reshape your understanding of your relationship with Him? Are you living in grateful dependence on His preservation, or in oblivious self-reliance?

Third Argument: Infinity of Souls and Its Refutation
Third argument and its refutation regarding infinite souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that posits an infinite number of souls and the response that avoids the impossibility of simultaneous infinity.

Core Idea
Infinite coexisting souls are possible if they lack natural or positional order.

The third argument claims that if souls are created with bodies, and bodies are infinite due to infinite celestial cycles, then souls would be infinite. Since bodies are successive, their infinity is fine, but souls persist after death, leading to an infinite number of coexisting souls, which is impossible. The author responds by stating that the impossibility requires order (natural or positional), but souls are not ordered, so infinity is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three parts: the argument about bodies, the problem of souls, and the response.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning.
Bodies are infinite due to celestial cycles. If souls are created with bodies, souls would be infinite. But bodies are successive, while souls persist, leading to infinite coexisting souls – impossible by the principle of correspondence.
3
Grasp the author's refutation.
The impossibility of infinite coexisting things requires ordering (natural or positional). Souls are not ordered, so their coexistence is allowed.
The key phrase: 'شرط امتناعه الترتب الطبيعي أو الوضعي'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion.
Thus, an infinite number of souls can exist simultaneously without contradiction.

Note: Aristotle on the Necessity of a Condition
Incomplete note on Aristotle's view of the condition for originated things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's statement that every originated thing requires a condition from an originated thing to depend on the eternal principle, but note the passage is incomplete.

Core Idea
Every originated thing needs a condition from an originated thing to avoid circularity.

This lesson covers a note attributed to Aristotle, which addresses the necessity of an originated condition for the emanation of originated things from the eternal principle. The passage is incomplete in the manuscript, so we present it as preserved, noting the missing end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it is a 'تنبيه' (note) from Aristotle, but it cuts off.
2
Understand the quoted statement.
Aristotle says every originated thing must depend on the eternal necessary principle through a condition from an originated thing.
3
Note the purpose.
The phrase 'دفعاً للدور والتسلسل' indicates this condition avoids circularity and infinite regress.
4
Recognize the incompleteness.
The passage ends abruptly; the full argument is not preserved. Be aware that this is a fragment.

?

The Nature of Jinn and Devils: Objections and Answers
Refutation of philosophical objections to jinn and devils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological doctrine of jinn and devils as bodies that can take any shape, and how the philosophers' objection about their subtlety is refuted by distinguishing two meanings of 'subtlety'.

Core Idea
Jinn are real, subtle bodies; their invisibility does not contradict their strength.

In this passage, the author concludes the section on intellects by discussing jinn and devils. He first affirms their existence as hidden bodies with special abilities, then addresses the philosophers' objection that such bodies would either be too weak or visible. The refutation hinges on a careful definition of 'subtlety'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice the author's structure: first a positive claim, then an objection, then the answer.
The passage uses a standard kalam style: claim, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the philosophers' objection
Find where the philosophers' argument begins. It has two horns: either the bodies are subtle or not. Understand the consequences of each.
The objection is a dilemma: both options seem impossible.
3
Study the answer: two meanings of subtlety
Focus on how the author answers by distinguishing two meanings of 'subtlety': transparency versus fragility. See how each horn is handled.
This is the key move: subtlety as transparency allows strength and invisibility.
4
Reflect on the final point about divine power
Read the last part about God bestowing power regardless of body constitution. Consider how this resolves the shape-shifting problem.
The argument appeals to observable examples (lion vs. donkey) to show strength is not tied to thickness.
